{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Who needs higher education when we have YouTube?","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/why-go-college-youtube-filmmaking","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODA5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzgyOTg1NH0.wIgmfvHsILCqeBWmc3cG3Mr_ODHylOU8PbDnqX9fJhg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C274&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Many famous directors never went to film school, claiming that creativity cannot be taught in a classroom setting. </li><li>Content creators on YouTube offer a Hollywood-certified film school education — for free. </li><li>Instead of using the internet to procrastinate, aspiring filmmakers are encouraged to test their knowledge in the real world.</li></ul><hr/><p>When New York University decided to close its classrooms back in March 2020, students were not sure how to feel. While most were more than willing to help check the spread of COVID-19, many worried that remote instruction would fall short of their standard tuition rates, which remain among the highest in the country.</p><p>These concerns were especially prevalent among film production majors, who require access to expensive tools like cameras, lights, and microphones, as well as in-person instruction to learn their complicated crafts. These resources raise the university's<a href=\"https://www.nyu.edu/admissions/financial-aid-and-scholarships/applying-and-planning-for-undergraduate-aid/tuition-and-other-costs/cost-of-attendance.html#tisch\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> average cost of attendance</a> for undergraduates from $56,500 to $62,062 a year.</p><p>With equipment stashed away inside campus vaults and glitchy Zoom meetings complicating the already herculean task of teaching someone how to set up a <a href=\"https://www.premiumbeat.com/blog/how-to-achieve-perfect-dolly-shot/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Dolly shot</a> when they are half a world away, it did not take long before reimbursement requests started flooding the email accounts of faculty administrators.</p><p>Initially, inquiries into possible refunds were met with a performance. Reminding students that the institution itself was bleeding, too, Tisch School of the Arts Dean Allyson Green shared a recording of herself dancing to \"Losing my Religion\" along with the message that \"great art will be made of this time, just as it has in times past.\"</p><p>Pandemic or not, film school has always had a tough time justifying its own existence. Aside from the fact that film school is as expensive as filmmaking itself, many famous directors — from Martin Scorsese to Quentin Tarantino — never went to film school and instead stand by the idea that creativity cannot be taught in a classroom.</p><p>Though passionate creatives find a way regardless of the hand they were dealt, these past two years have prompted students, schools, and employers to take a closer look at the potential of remote learning, and aspiring filmmakers found that YouTube offers many options for learning screenwriting, cinematography, and editing. All for free.</p><h2>A world-class education on YouTube</h2><p>If you want to receive an undergraduate degree from NYU's Kanbar Institute of Film and Television, which <em>The Hollywood Reporter</em> ranked as the<a href=\"https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/lists/top-25-american-film-schools-ranked-1134785/1-usc-2/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> second-best program</a> of its kind in America, you would need to complete coursework in three categories: production, writing, and history and criticism, the last of which roughly translates to cinema studies.</p><p>Let's start with cinema studies because these lecture-based courses easily lend themselves to online alternatives. Obviously, you will need to watch a lot of movies and TV if you want to become a filmmaker — not just contemporary hits but classics as well, preferably from all around the world rather than just the bowels of Hollywood.</p><p>When composing a watch list, students should look for authoritative sources. These include rankings from respected organizations like the<a href=\"https://www.bfi.org.uk/sight-and-sound/greatest-films-all-time\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> British Film Institute</a>,<a href=\"https://www.wga.org/writers-room/101-best-lists/101-greatest-screenplays\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Writers Guild of America</a>, and the<a href=\"https://www.thegreatestfilms.com/List/Directors-Guild-of-America-DGA/The-80-Best-Directed-Films\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Directors Guild of America</a>, not to mention esteemed trade publications such as<a href=\"https://www.filmcomment.com/best-films-of-the-decade/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <em>Film Comment</em></a> and<a href=\"https://mubi.com/lists/cahiers-du-cinema-top-100\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <em>Cahiers du Cinéma</em></a>.</p><p>While these listmakers are highly respected within the industry, they also form part of an outdated film establishment. For more culturally diverse selections, look no further than the YouTube channel<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCVtL1edhT8qqY-j2JIndMzg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Cinefix</a>, which uploads encyclopedic overviews of the most original, most beautiful, and most groundbreaking films.</p><p>If watching great movies constitutes the first part of cinema studies, figuring out what makes them so great must be the second. As far as independent content creators go, filmmakers Taylor Ramos and Tony Zhou are without a doubt the most skilled and eloquent film instructors you will be able to find on the internet.</p><p>With over 28 videos ranging from the ways in which Hong Kong stuntman Jackie Chan choreographs action scenes to Buster Keaton's influence on visual comedy, their popular YouTube channel<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCjFqcJQXGZ6T6sxyFB-5i6A\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Every Frame a Painting</a> provides a crash course in editing, cinematography, sound design, and much, much more.</p><h2>A YouTube education curriculum</h2>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"5626fc5edb84aff586da68ef9492f5f6\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"d5752\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODEwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NDA1NDExMH0.0qZkEJz8jc-ntHHw-Q3kHBzgZW-rtLRctzxwYz2zzDY/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://unsplash.com/photos/J39X2xX_8CQ\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Jeremy Yap via Unsplash</small></p>\n        <p>Up next is writing, which is tougher to teach as every writer has their own near-religious notions of what a proper story should look like. If Charlie Kaufman's self-referential film <em>Adaptation</em> taught us anything, it is that screenwriters should not blindly follow the dictates of imposing Hollywood script gurus like Robert McKee.<br/></p><p>In truth, they would be better off listening to advice from the soft-spoken Michael Tucker, whose channel<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/c/LessonsfromtheScreenplay\" target=\"_blank\"> Lessons From the Screenplay</a> (LFTS) breaks down the framework behind <em>Adaptation</em>, <em>Inglourious Basterds</em>, <em>Get Out</em>, and other films better than stone-cold writing manuals like McKee's <em>Story</em> ever could.</p><p>There are actually quite a lot of narrative focused video essayists worth checking out, if only for their different tastes and perspectives. Aside from LFTS, you also have<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCx0L2ZdYfiq-tsAXb8IXpQg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Just Write</a>,<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/c/HelloFutureMe\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Hello Future Me</a>,<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/c/NandovMovies\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Nando v Movies</a> and<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/nerdwriter1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Nerdwriter1</a>, each of which analyzes noteworthy films of all kinds, from hidden gems to action blockbusters.</p><p>As helpful as these channels may be, however, the majority of them are owned and operated by people who have spent their careers critiquing movies rather than actually writing them. To that end, the most valuable channel of all may just be the least known one: an up-and-coming channel called<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC3osNjJeuDdvyALIEP-nh0g\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Behind the Curtain</a>.</p><p>The premise is simple: rather than relying on commentary from story analysts, this channel sifts the internet to piece together the profoundest tidbits of advice uttered by some of the industry's finest filmmakers, from Vince Giligan and Aaron Sorken to Christopher Nolan and the Safdie Brothers.</p><h2>Learning how to use equipment — also on YouTube</h2><p>While reviewing films and writing screenplays can easily be done alone at home, getting familiar with industry equipment cannot. For this reason, production and post-production are the most difficult subjects to teach online. Still, despite the many challenges involved, YouTubers have found ways to make it work. </p><p>Whether you are trying to do something as simple as changing a lightbulb or as complicated as building your own personal nuclear reactor, chances are someone somewhere has uploaded a YouTube video showing you exactly how to do it, and the core components of filmmaking are no exception to this unwritten rule.</p><p>Where<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCc0D1yh3TGE8dJSLm9VmEdg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Aputure</a> and<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC6xliqRXcbqk6vU_GxaITQQ\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Grip Tips</a> offer tutorials for individual lights and camera models,<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCjHg96xQd03z-xXgMBRwYzw\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Hurlbut Academy</a> and<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/c/TheSlantedLens\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> The Slanted Lens</a> show viewers how to incorporate these tools into particular set layouts.<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCDBV1_28Q_NKF2SxnLO-3jg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Deity Microphones</a> is a great source for everything sound related, from best positions to hold a boom pole to how to mic a moving car.</p><p>Once you have learned to jump, it is time to fly. Channels like<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC7gBcKzHKc0oOR5-XiWdlFQ\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> wolfcrow</a> and<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCmhVYUCCOm0D5bT12MOr5_w\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Shutterstock</a> explain how some of the techniques analyzed by the aforementioned content creators can be implemented using combinations of equipment. Along the way,<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/indymogul\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Indy Mogul</a> discusses tricks to keep your productions cheap as well as efficient.</p><p>Inside the editing room, footage shot on set is broken down and reassembled. Before aspiring filmmakers are able to play with their images, however, they will have to learn how to use a number of complicated software applications. While this may seem like an insurmountable task at first, it is nothing a simple step-by-step guide cannot fix.</p><p>The entertainment industry uses hundreds of applications, and whatever combination of them you end up using depends on the niche in which you establish yourself. That said, two of the most widely used for post-production jobs are Adobe Premiere (used to edit films) and After Effects (used to add, well, aftereffects). </p><p>As far as Premiere tutorials go, you cannot get more detailed than that of<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/Justthisgood\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Justin Odisho</a>, whose playlists consist of nearly 290 videos, taking young editors from basic concepts to advanced methods. YouTuber<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/user/SonduckFilm\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> SonduckFilm</a> does the same thing but for After Effects, across a whopping 440 separate installments no less.</p><h2>DIY Filmmaking</h2><p>Once you have watched each and every one of these YouTubers, you will inevitably come face to face with the question, \"What's next?\" As informative as the internet can be, it often enables us to procrastinate. When trying to learn something off YouTube, therefore, it is paramount that you do more than just binge watch videos.</p><p>Instead of falling down bottomless internet rabbit holes, aspiring filmmakers should go out into the real world and put the knowledge gained from all those content creators to the test. Around the country, film festivals offer opportunities for struggling artists to display their work and build their resume.</p><p>Aside from festivals, government agencies, nonprofit organizations, creative fellowships, and development labs offer funds and resources that give people the extra push they need to finish that script that they have been working on or secure funding for that production they have been meaning to organize.</p><p>The second question — whether it is worth putting yourself through film school — is a lot more difficult to answer, especially since the events of 2020 have breathed new life into the discussion. Will checking out any of these YouTubers turn you into the next George Lucas? Probably not, but a degree from CalArts cannot guarantee it either.</p><p>Ryan Koo, founder of<a href=\"https://nofilmschool.com/u/ryan\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> No Film School</a>, once said he built his free, online database not because he wanted to discourage anybody from attending film school, but because he wanted to provide crucial resources to people who did not have the time or money to attend film school.</p><p>In a similar vein, I do not argue that applying to film school months after the zenith of a global pandemic is a poor decision to make. Rather, I want to show aspiring filmmakers that they do not have to go through formal and expensive programs to pursue their passion.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/58ODdkRA6Ew\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Aug 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODA5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzgyOTg1NH0.wIgmfvHsILCqeBWmc3cG3Mr_ODHylOU8PbDnqX9fJhg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C274&height=700","linkMd5":"5345321b556c200b513fa64d6b5c6141","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn39@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-291_9d889a5a256b1478.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":114492,"destBytes":114492,"author":"Tim Brinkhof","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODA5OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzgyOTg1NH0.wIgmfvHsILCqeBWmc3cG3Mr_ODHylOU8PbDnqX9fJhg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C108%2C0%2C274&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn39@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-291_9d889a5a256b1478.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODEwNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NDA1NDExMH0.0qZkEJz8jc-ntHHw-Q3kHBzgZW-rtLRctzxwYz2zzDY/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn11@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-281_d575282c7f808c20.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/58ODdkRA6Ew":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn25@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-41-997_321a64710684809e.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"A gruesome death: the macabre science of dehydration","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/gruesome-death-macabre-science-dehydration","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMTU4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1Njg3NDQ3Nn0.gC7MEHWnVRMpn17kushgVhOdg5UJhQaGYvANJckFSD8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>When our bodies are deprived of water, they take water from other organs, ultimately leading to organ failure and brain shrinkage.</li><li>Dehydration is considered one of the most painful and protracted deaths a human can experience.</li><li>Every year, more athletes die from overhydration than dehydration.</li></ul><hr/><p>In 1994, the Italian athlete <a href=\"https://www.google.com/amp/s/www.bbc.com/news/magazine-30046426.amp\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Mauro Prosperi</a> entered one of the most intense and grueling endurance races in the world: the Marathon des Sables. This six-day ultra-marathon takes place in the Sahara desert, and it pushes people to the limits of their mental and physical abilities. The temperatures of the race can reach up to 50 C° (122 F°), and the average runner drinks 13 liters (3.4 gallons) per day, most of which comes straight out as sweat. </p><p>But for Mauro, things were even worse than that. After a sandstorm, the former Olympian was caught alone for ten days in the desert without a support team or supplies to help him through. Over the course of his wandering, Mauro stayed alive by storing his earliest urine in bottles — since this had the highest water content — and drinking it later on. He killed and ate twenty bats and drank their blood in the hope of staying hydrated. Luckily, it worked just long enough. Eventually, he came across a goat herder, and his long recovery began. </p><p>But in such a short time, his body was wrecked by dehydration. His eyes were nearly ruined and his liver was seriously damaged. He could not eat anything other than soup or water for weeks, and it took him two years to fully recover. </p><h2>Walking bags of water</h2><p>It has long been known that hydration is essential to a properly functioning body. Our bodies are 50 to 70 percent water, which means an average man will contain up to 40 liters (10.6 gallons). This can differ on any given day, and it largely explains why our body weight also can fluctuate by about 2 kg (5 pounds) per day. </p><p>It is recommended that we each drink roughly 3 liters (0.8 gallon) of water a day to replace the stuff we lose through breathing, sweating, and urinating. While this seems like a lot, for most of us, that water comes from drinks like coffee or soda, and up to 20 percent of it can come from the food we eat. Yet, on some days, we just do not drink enough, and it shows.</p><p>When we are mildly dehydrated, our bodies start to creak. We get headaches because, without water, the brain will contract ever so slightly, causing it to pull away from the skull. We get constipated because our body draws needed water from the large intestine. We get tired and find it hard to focus because, without water, our blood literally thickens, meaning the body has to work harder to get nutrients and oxygen to the organs. </p><p>It is thought that even a 2 percent drop in hydration levels can noticeably impede performance in a variety of areas. Usually, this can be easily resolved, but what happens if you continue to dehydrate? And why is it thought to be one of the most painful and protracted deaths that we can experience?</p><h2>A gruesome death by dehydration</h2><p>Imagine for a moment that someone was to lock the doors where you are right now. Assuming you had no emergency bottles of water, it would take you roughly seven days to die from dehydration. If you were lost in the desert with no water at all, it would take a day and a half.</p><p>When the body is forced into extreme situations like heat, cold, or water deprivation, it makes a tactical decision to withdraw resources from the least essential parts first. With dehydration, this initially happens in the kidneys. Our kidneys will reabsorb water that would have been used in urine, so this is why your urine gets darker when you are dehydrated — the urea concentration increases. </p><p>When this is still not enough, the body will draw water from your cells and organs to retain the necessary blood pressure to keep you ticking. Your eyes will contract to expose the conjunctiva, your lips will shrivel away entirely, your teeth and gums will project outward like on a skeleton, your skin blackens and dries out, and your tongue becomes a tiny piece of beef jerky. If you are cut, you are too dry to even bleed.</p><p>Only after days of this slow tortue will you die. With less and less water, the blood in your body will thicken, and your blood pressure will drop drastically. This means that all of the oxygen and nutrients in your blood will take a lot longer to get to your organs, and so they become deprived. Your brain, heart, kidneys, and liver begin to fail. Ultimately, your brain will start to shrink from osmosis in an attempt to hydrate the body (the brain has a lot of water), which eventually will cause you to die.</p><h2>A finely tuned machine</h2><p>When blood volume changes — that is, decreases through dehydration or increases through overhydration — the careful balance of electrolytes is disrupted. These electrolytes (potassium and sodium, mainly) are responsible for muscle contraction and conducting nerve signals, so an imbalance is extremely serious and potentially lethal. </p><p>This is why rehydration needs to be treated as carefully as dehydration. In 2007, fitness instructor David Rogers died after finishing the London Marathon, not because he drank too little but because he drank far too much. His was far from the only such death. Today, it is thought that more athletes die from overhydration than dehydration. </p><p>The problem is that when we feel really thirsty, it can take a while for the body (specifically, the hypothalamus in the brain) to register that it has enough water. If someone drinks <a href=\"https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/av/uk-england-hampshire-50170837\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">5 liters (1.3 gallons) of wate</a>r in a short space of time, the body will whisk the fluid away to cells far too quickly. This causes sodium levels to become diluted (a state known as \"hyponatremia\"), which in turn causes organs like the lungs and brain to take on too much water and swell dangerously. This could cause you to faint or even die.</p><p>Hyponatremia highlights just how fragile our body's homeostatic balance really is. While the human body can endure a great many things — and it really is hugely adaptable most of the time — it is also in many ways incredibly fragile. Even a day without a glass of water can impede our performance. After a few more days, you will be dead. </p><p>So, the next time you notice your urine darkening, remember that this is the first step your body is taking to prevent serious organ damage.</p><p><em>Jonny Thomson teaches philosophy in Oxford. He runs a popular Instagram account called Mini Philosophy (@</em><a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/philosophyminis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>philosophyminis</em></a><em>). His first book is </em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Mini-Philosophy-Small-Book-Ideas-ebook/dp/B08M3XDNPM/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Mini Philosophy: A Small Book of Big Ideas</em></a><em>.</em></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/2q0hqgEXlBo\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Aug 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMTU4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1Njg3NDQ3Nn0.gC7MEHWnVRMpn17kushgVhOdg5UJhQaGYvANJckFSD8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"c951465f86505325bb28a06437fbaeef","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn34@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-22-994_376572222390c467.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":227188,"destBytes":227188,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMTU4OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1Njg3NDQ3Nn0.gC7MEHWnVRMpn17kushgVhOdg5UJhQaGYvANJckFSD8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn34@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-22-994_376572222390c467.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/2q0hqgEXlBo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn99@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-28-445_597b72ba485e614b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588786},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The healing power of love? Pair-bonding might prevent cancer in mice","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/widowhood-effect","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE5MDc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjM5NTk2MX0.H5O3CFF63_l4FvgdPjJJ09ToE2seHO1VLxxF7wZLxWc/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C349%2C0%2C350&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>The widowhood effect describes how someone becomes more likely to die shortly after the death of their spouse.</li><li>In the new study, the results of rodent experiments suggest the existence of a biological mechanism by which pair-bonding changes the gene expression of cancer cells.</li><li>The results could illuminate new avenues for cancer treatments, but they also raise concerns over the accuracy of medical research involving rodents.</li></ul><hr/><p>Does love possess healing powers? The question might seem naïve, but it is one <a href=\"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/27569105/\" target=\"_blank\">physicians have contemplated in various forms for nearly two millennia</a>.</p><p>The idea that love heals might have originated from its inverse: sadness harms. In the 2nd century, the Greek physician Aelius Galenus proposed that melancholy and depression can cause cancer. Other physicians would go on to repeat this hypothesis, including Arab doctors in the 9th century, French physicians in the 17th century, and the 20th century American psychologist Elida Evans, whose 1926 review of 100 cancer patients found that all had lost important emotional relationships prior to developing the disease. </p><p>The results of a new study suggest that loving relationships do indeed protect against cancer. What's more, this protection does <em>not </em>seem to stem from the behavioral or lifestyle traits of couples but rather from a biological mechanism that directly inhibits tumor growth. </p><h2>The widowhood effect</h2><p>Published in the journal <a href=\"https://elifesciences.org/articles/64711\" target=\"_blank\"><em>eLife</em></a>, the study used mice to explore the \"widowhood effect,\" a well-documented phenomenon in which someone becomes more likely to die shortly after the death of their spouse. </p><p>The researchers extracted blood sera from two groups of mice. One group comprised monogamous mice that had bonded for one year; the other had their pair bonds disrupted after 12 months. The researchers then grew human lung cancer cells in sera from both groups. In the blood of mice with disrupted pair bonds, cancer cells grew larger, took on shapes linked to an increased risk of cancer, and showed gene activity that suggested an increased ability to spread. </p><p>The researchers conducted a second experiment within live mice. They extracted lung cancer cells from pair-bonded and pair-disrupted mice and implanted the cells into virgin mice with weakened immune systems. The cancer cells from pair-disrupted mice grew more effectively in the virgin rodents, suggesting that \"the protective effects of pair bonding persist even after removal from the original mouse.\"</p><h2>Love heals all wounds</h2><p>Overall, the results suggest that social activity — particularly pair bonding, e.g. positive and intimate relationships — can change the gene expression and growth of tumors. But how? It is not exactly clear, but the authors of an article published in <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/trends/molecular-medicine/fulltext/S1471-4914(21)00195-7\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Trends in Molecular Medicine</em></a> proposed a three-step process:</p><ol><li>Social information is encoded in a neural signal.</li><li>The neural signal directly or indirectly induces the release of some sort of immune-related factor into the bloodstream. </li><li>The humoral factor binds to a receptor on cancer cells that induces changes in gene expression.</li></ol><p>If correct, this process could change how researchers think about the widowhood effect, which is often explained by lifestyle changes, hormone-induced changes to the heart, or disregarded as coincidence. The new model could establish a biological basis for the effect, one that illuminates avenues for novel cancer treatments in humans — if the same processes are observed in people. </p><p>The researchers wrote: </p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"...cancers at widowhood represent a distinct pathological entity that may deserve targeted therapeutic strategies, which should take into consideration social interactions. Thus, preventive measures could be developed to mitigate such pro-oncogenic effects in individuals at bereavement.\"</p><p>It's hard to say what those treatments would look like. After all, even though research has linked pair-bonding to <a href=\"https://healthcare.utah.edu/healthfeed/postings/2017/02/relationships.php\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">numerous health benefits</a> — longer lifespan, lower blood pressure, improved mental health — doctors cannot prescribe love. Pharmacological treatments would probably need to do the job.</p><p>The researchers also noted that the results raise concerns over medical studies that utilize animal models: given that the health of mice may partly depend on the rodents' own relationships, it is possible that some studies failed to \"accurately capture the whole spectrum of the tumorigenic process and the associated host-derived factors.\"</p><div></div><ul></ul><div></div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BQtxLK3Fwxw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Aug 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE5MDc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjM5NTk2MX0.H5O3CFF63_l4FvgdPjJJ09ToE2seHO1VLxxF7wZLxWc/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C349%2C0%2C350&height=700","linkMd5":"b5cebaf8ff4a0ce77dae2d446a841a17","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn43@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-222_16726247dfc97622.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":107686,"destBytes":107686,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE5MDc3NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjM5NTk2MX0.H5O3CFF63_l4FvgdPjJJ09ToE2seHO1VLxxF7wZLxWc/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C349%2C0%2C350&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn43@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-222_16726247dfc97622.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BQtxLK3Fwxw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn37@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-984_a0cbf894fc5ef57b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588781},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The most terrifying pterosaur yet is discovered","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/thapunngaka-shawi","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDcyOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MDc5NTE0OH0.cEmRyumJxmXNB8axcD5h7SJS2t-kCEFE87Dq8JPz3Y0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Australian scientists identify a fossil as a previously unknown pterosaur.</li><li><span></span>The pterosaur was large and savage.</li><li>Only a part of its jaw was found, but that was enough to draw several conclusions about it.</li></ul><hr/><p>Who hasn't wondered what it would be like to live in the era of the dinosaurs? It certainly would be an amazing sight — but also utterly terrifying, once the reality set in that you were rather close to the bottom of the food chain, a tasty morsel for tyrannosaurs and pterosaurs alike.</p><p>Now we know, thanks to University of Queensland researchers, that the Cretaceous was even scarier than we thought: they have announced the discovery of perhaps the most fearsome pterosaur of all, <em>Thapunngaka shawi</em>. The study is published in the <a href=\"https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/02724634.2021.1946068?src=\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Journal of Vertebrate Paleontology</em></a>.</p><p>\"This thing would have been quite savage,\" says Tim Richards of the university's <a href=\"https://dinosaurs.group.uq.edu.au/\" target=\"_blank\">Dinosaur Lab</a>, one of the authors of the study announcing your new nightmare. \"It would have cast a great shadow over some quivering little dinosaur that wouldn't have heard it until it was too late.\"</p><h2>Shaw's spear mouth</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"2ac22c86336889e1f30f4f3fa29c3d57\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"f66dd\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDc0OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NjY2ODg4MX0.PWDU5GmpS7DtGJQBdrJjMO4Nre2ZFq8uwltqLDqDdlc/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Reconstruction of the skull of Thapunngaka shawi</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: Tim Richards / <a href=\"https://dinosaurs.group.uq.edu.au/article/2021/08/spear-mouthed-pterosaur-soared-over-dinosaurs-outback-queensland\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">University of Queensland</a></small></p><p>The find is remarkable. Pterosaurs had light, relatively hollow, thin-walled bones that did not lend themselves to fossilization. \"It's quite amazing fossils of these animals exist at all,\" says Richards, who also notes that, \"By world standards, the Australian pterosaur record is poor, but the discovery of <em>Thapunngaka</em> contributes greatly to our understanding of Australian pterosaur diversity.\"<br/></p><p>Local fossil collector Len Shaw came across the fossil in a quarry northwest of Richmond, Australia back in 2011. It is not an entire pterosaur specimen but rather just a <a href=\"https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/rostral\" target=\"_blank\">rostral</a> portion of a crested <a href=\"https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/mandible\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">mandible</a> from which the scientists were eventually able to extrapolate more of the creature's features. Geologically speaking, it was found in the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toolebuc_Formation\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Toolebuc Formation</a>.</p><p>The pterosaur's genus name is dedicated to the now-extinct Wanamara indigenous people who lived in the area. (The species name belongs to its discoverer.) Senior author Steve Salisbury parses out the moniker: \"The genus name, <em>Thapunngaka</em>, incorporates thapun [ta-boon] and ngaka [nga-ga], the Wanamara words for 'spear' and 'mouth', respectively.\"</p><p>In other words, the pterosaur's name means \"Shaw's spear mouth.\"</p><h2>Made for predation</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"a45b54fc2c8d2871b4d23dcb8eaed240\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"a47d5\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDc1NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzkwNzc3MH0.FKfquwVLROKffkVNzy-xGb_ih2Ncv0V_AG84bMDIJDM/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Thapunngaka shawi compared to a man,<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mythunga\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Mythunga camara</a>, and Ferrodraco lentoni</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: T. Richards / <a href=\"https://dinosaurs.group.uq.edu.au/article/2021/08/spear-mouthed-pterosaur-soared-over-dinosaurs-outback-queensland\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">University of Queensland</a></small></p><p>Pterosaurs should not be mistaken for ancient birds. \"Even though pterosaurs could fly, they were nothing like a bird, or even a bat — Pterosaurs were a successful and diverse group of reptiles — the very first back-boned animals to take a stab at powered flight.\"<br/></p><p><em>Thapunngaka shawi</em> was an <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anhanguera_(pterosaur)\" target=\"_blank\">anhanguerian</a>, a type of pterosaur that dominated all of Earth's continents in the latter days of the dinosaurs. <em>T. shawi</em>, says Richards, \"would have been a fearsome beast, with a spear-like mouth and a wingspan around seven meters. It was essentially just a skull with a long neck, bolted on a pair of long wings.\"</p><p>Also teeth. Lots of teeth. The one-meter-long head sported about 40 of them. Researchers figure that they were well-suited to plucking fish from Queensland's long-ago <a href=\"https://www.earthbyte.org/modelling-the-evolution-of-the-eromanga-sea-in-the-context-of-tectonics-geodynamics-and-surface-processes/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Eromanga Sea</a>.</p><p>Salisbury notes that <em>Thapunngaka shawi</em> had a bony crest on its lower jaw that is larger than that of any other anhanguerian found so far. It may have had one on top as well. \"These crests probably played a role in the flight dynamics of these creatures,\" says Salisbury, \"and hopefully future research will deliver more definitive answers.\"</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/9GBh8zN4M2s\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Aug 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDcyOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MDc5NTE0OH0.cEmRyumJxmXNB8axcD5h7SJS2t-kCEFE87Dq8JPz3Y0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"0d8184896770e150a17d921bf841faa9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn5@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-172_b2879ec4983d4dda.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":36902,"destBytes":36902,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDcyOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MDc5NTE0OH0.cEmRyumJxmXNB8axcD5h7SJS2t-kCEFE87Dq8JPz3Y0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn5@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-172_b2879ec4983d4dda.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDc0OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NjY2ODg4MX0.PWDU5GmpS7DtGJQBdrJjMO4Nre2ZFq8uwltqLDqDdlc/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn39@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-079_f66dd308a643ec52.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDc1NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzkwNzc3MH0.FKfquwVLROKffkVNzy-xGb_ih2Ncv0V_AG84bMDIJDM/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn58@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-28-008_a47d50bc899c630b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/9GBh8zN4M2s":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn96@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-264_854aec60d4560018.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"One man visited all 2964 bus stops in San Francisco — for science","link":"https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/san-francisco-bus-stops","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDkzMS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NzYyNTM0MH0.As6AdLaVu3beXL14TJrUJSVlcbOClAqGsXp_1Iut06Q/img.png?width=1245&amp;coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&amp;height=700\" />\n<br />\n<br />\n<ul> \n <li>In three months last year, Marcel Moran visited all of San Francisco's bus stops.</li>\n <li>His aim: to map the quality of their signage, seating, shelter, and other amenities.</li>\n <li>Correcting the imbalances he found could be the key to increasing ridership.</li>\n</ul>\n<hr />\n<p><br /></p>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"8ac5db5165e7a1dea749d70c9326eb3a\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"2b5ce\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDkzOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTE3ODAwNH0.G8pOYxhvDjjtUGnSSx6-8SV2rFuoqqQeny5bM6RqRYc/img.jpg?width=980\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">A bus stop in central San Francisco with proper signage, seating, and shelter. About 10 percent of bus stops in the city are so underdeveloped that they are, for all practical purposes, invisible. </small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.dk/detail/news-photo/passengers-board-a-san-francisco-muni-bus-during-the-news-photo/1217248463?adppopup=true\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Justin Sullivan/Getty Images </small></p>\n<p>The San Francisco County Municipal Transportation Authority (SFMTA) has nearly 3,000 bus stops in its network. Over three months in mid-2020, Marcel Moran visited every last one of them.</p>\n<h2>Not just a weird hobby</h2>\n<p>It would have been one of the weirder ways to kill time during the first months of the pandemic, but Mr. Moran's reasons were more noteworthy than that. His was the first comprehensive survey of San Francisco's bus stop amenities — more precisely, the level of comfort and security that they provide.</p>\n<p>Previous studies show that the presence of seats, shelter, and even nearby trees make waiting for the bus seem shorter. Also, electronic screens showing estimated times of arrival (ETAs) reduce the frustration of waiting.</p>\n<p>This suggests that a well-furnished bus stop will reassure riders and can even attract more. On the other hand, a shoddy, neglected bus stop will do the opposite. Accordingly, a number of transit agencies aim to improve the quality of their bus stops. However, what's missing from their approach is a comprehensive study of bus-stop amenities to underpin such a strategy.</p>\n<p>Or it was, until now. Mr Moran, a Ph.D. candidate in city planning at Berkeley, has produced a \"census\" of bus stop amenities throughout San Francisco — the first standardized overview of its kind. For each of the stops, he wanted to know: Is it properly indicated? Does it offer seating and/or shelter? Is there an electronic message board with real-time traffic info — and does it work properly? And is the access from curb to bus unobstructed by parked cars? <br /></p>\n<h2>No seating, no shelter, no signage — no riders?</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"a120ecfbb1d7c47177b90e4d214526b4\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"9f8b4\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk0OC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzM5NzgxM30.ciTqVA1W6iAd1pUVqUmT_GObyphL9TNOyo1vCMxsWPI/img.png?width=980\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">Only 34 percent of bus stops in San Francisco offer seating (white), while 66 percent do not (red). </small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://digitalcommons.usf.edu/jpt/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Marcel Moran / Journal of Public Transportation – <a href=\"https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-NC 4.0</a></small></p>\n<p>In a number of ways, San Francisco is an ideal place to test the quality of public transport amenities. The city is something of a laboratory for mobility innovation — see experiments with dynamic parking prices, shared bicycle and e-scooter programs, and a ban on private cars along Market Street.</p>\n<p>Already in 1973, San Francisco declared itself a \"transit first\" city, meaning that city policies would favor public transport over private cars. The policy remains in place today, although this will come as a surprise to many who use the city's public bus network. </p>\n<p>Because Mr Moran's survey found that:</p>\n<ul>\n <li>Only 34 percent of San Francisco's bus stops offer seating of some kind.</li>\n <li>Only 31 percent featured shelter from the elements.</li>\n <li>At 32 percent of bus stops, on-street parking obstructed curbside access to the buses.</li>\n <li>Only 30 percent displayed a route map, usually as part of a shelter.</li>\n <li>Only 23 percent of bus stops had ETA screens (and they did not work at 2 percent of stops).</li>\n <li>11 percent did not appear to have any kind of visible signage at all.</li>\n <li>Some bus routes have seating at 75 percent of stops, while others no more than 10 percent.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>The study's findings, which appear in an article in the upcoming issue of the <em>Journal of Public Transportation</em>, not only point to large shortcomings in San Francisco's bus stop amenities but also reveal a glaring geographical gap. While \"a majority of (San Francisco) bus stops lack both seating and shelter of any kind,\" Mr. Moran remarks that \"amenity 'coldspots' nearly all lie within the city's southern half.\"<br /></p>\n<h2>North vs. South</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"a53aa6708909c101462bf660e04181f0\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"d8ee4\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk1Ni9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjA5MjcxOH0.y2f9X4Q2cr9COnXuIRPz7esYffsrymM-LdFrwgghgRQ/img.png?width=980\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">Barrier-free access from curb to bus is almost a given in the north; you are much more likely to have to navigate a row of parked cars in the south.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://digitalcommons.usf.edu/jpt/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Marcel Moran / Journal of Public Transportation – <a href=\"https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-NC 4.0</a></small></p>\n<p>The comfort level of bus stops is generally much higher in the northern half of the city, the location of its central and commercial districts. It is much lower in the south, which is more residential and peripheral.</p>\n<ul>\n <li>In the north, 45 percent of bus stops had seating, and 42 percent offered shelter.</li>\n <li>Also, more than 80 percent provided curb-to-bus access unhindered by on-street parking.</li>\n <li>In the south, only 22 percent of bus stops had seating, with the same share of the total offering shelter.</li>\n <li>The level of barrier-free bus access was also lower, at just 53 percent.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>It will surprise few that there are socioeconomic and racial components to the imbalance, with the north of the city generally being wealthier and whiter than the south, which is poorer and with more people of color.</p>\n<p>Public transport, including by bus, is key to reducing the congestion and pollution caused by urban traffic. Yet bus ridership in the U.S. has been on a slow and steady decline for years — and a precipitous one since the start of the pandemic.</p>\n<h2>Reversing the trend via the \"social minimum\"</h2>\n<p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\"> <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"301667f5fc0601150c1856832d777d71\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"bdadc\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk1Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzODU3OTEyOH0.SgrJ_j_F8BnP6AWFLYB10O1_T63v2gNm9AnuqidSvCA/img.jpg?width=980\" /> <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\" spellcheck=\"false\">These maps show hotspots (in red) and coldspots (in blue) of the availability of seating (left) and obstruction-free access (right) at bus stops in San Francisco.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://digitalcommons.usf.edu/jpt/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: Marcel Moran / Journal of Public Transportation – <a href=\"https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">CC BY-NC 4.0</a></small></p>\n<p>Reversing that trend will require raising the quality of public transport, but in most such considerations, the amenities provided by bus stops play little or no part. Mr Moran's study could help change that. \"When parking is abundant and free, and bus stops in many neighborhoods are genuinely hard to spot and uncomfortable to wait at, we sift out many would-be riders,\" he told <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-08-03/how-better-bus-stops-can-boost-transit-ridership?utm_content=citylab&amp;utm_campaign=socialflow-organic&amp;utm_medium=social&amp;utm_source=twitter\" target=\"_blank\">CityLab</a>.</p>\n<p>In his article, he advocates the social minimum principle, which would require a minimum level of amenities at all bus stops within the network. Approached by CityLab, the SFMTA said it was aware of \"imbalances\" in bus stop quality but planned to provide uniform signage for all, making them more visible and user friendly. No word, however, on seating, shelter and barrier-free access.</p>\n<p><em>For more on Marcel Moran's study, see his article: </em>Are Shelters in Place?: Mapping the Distribution of Transit Amenities via a Bus-Stop Census of San Francisco<em>, cited </em><a href=\"https://escholarship.org/content/qt3gj1t495/qt3gj1t495_noSplash_414b4583354f5a46ca2dd75ba0ad7a1d.pdf\" style=\"text-decoration-line: line-through;\" target=\"_blank\">here</a><em> by the Berkeley Institute of Transportation Studies, and soon in the </em><a href=\"https://digitalcommons.usf.edu/jpt/\" target=\"_blank\">Journal of Public Transportation</a><em> (Vol. 23, No. 3 – July 2021).</em></p>\n<p><em>Also, check out </em><a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/citylab\" target=\"_blank\">Bloomberg CityLab</a><em> for interesting stuff on urban design issues — plus some cool cartography content in the sub-series MapLab.</em></p>\n<p><strong>Strange Maps #1099</strong></p>\n<p><em>Got a strange map?
