+0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDkzMS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NzYyNTM0MH0.As6AdLaVu3beXL14TJrUJSVlcbOClAqGsXp_1Iut06Q/img.png?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"5c5d6337cf9ab97e1f078042e1387b7b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn30@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-27-450_5092808ccb1a3d25.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":89386,"destBytes":89386,"author":"Frank Jacobs","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDkzMS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NzYyNTM0MH0.As6AdLaVu3beXL14TJrUJSVlcbOClAqGsXp_1Iut06Q/img.png?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn30@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-27-450_5092808ccb1a3d25.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDkzOC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTE3ODAwNH0.G8pOYxhvDjjtUGnSSx6-8SV2rFuoqqQeny5bM6RqRYc/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn11@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-28-005_2b5ce5011249032a.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk0OC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzMzM5NzgxM30.ciTqVA1W6iAd1pUVqUmT_GObyphL9TNOyo1vCMxsWPI/img.png?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn23@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-977_9f8b4e5258d8925a.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk1Ni9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2MjA5MjcxOH0.y2f9X4Q2cr9COnXuIRPz7esYffsrymM-LdFrwgghgRQ/img.png?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn3@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-272_d8ee49991d18780d.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDk1Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzODU3OTEyOH0.SgrJ_j_F8BnP6AWFLYB10O1_T63v2gNm9AnuqidSvCA/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn7@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-271_bdadcfff9ac85526.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/6mAbjbfDTb4":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn35@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-27-979_f60bf6dfe43f65f1.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588788},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"How to take Earth’s inner temperature with erupted sea glass","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/earth-temperature","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDg0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NTcwODExNn0.-zyzNImMW63E9O7IKsdLbsRM9oxnyVQu87xiH52TrrY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C1230%2C0%2C1032&height=700\"/><br/><br/>If the Earth's oceans were drained completely, they would reveal a massive chain of undersea volcanoes snaking around the planet.<hr/><p> This sprawling ocean ridge system is a product of overturning material in the Earth's interior, where boiling temperatures can melt and loft rocks up through the crust, splitting the sea floor and reshaping the planet's surface over hundreds of millions of years.</p><p>Now geologists at MIT have analyzed thousands of samples of erupted material along ocean ridges and traced back their chemical history to estimate the temperature of the Earth's interior.</p><p>Their analysis shows that the temperature of the Earth's underlying ocean ridges is relatively consistent, at around 1,350 degrees Celsius — about as hot as a gas range's blue flame. There are, however, \"hotspots\" along the ridge that can reach 1,600 degrees Celsius, comparable to the hottest lava.</p><p>The team's results, appearing in the <em>Journal of Geophysical Research: Solid Earth, </em>provide a temperature map of the Earth's interior around ocean ridges. With this map, scientists can better understand the melting processes that give rise to undersea volcanoes, and how these processes may drive the pace of plate tectonics over time.</p><p>\"Convection and plate tectonics have been important processes in shaping Earth history,\" says lead author Stephanie Brown Krein, a postdoc in MIT's Department of Earth, Atmospheric and Planetary Sciences (EAPS). \"Knowing the temperature along this whole chain is fundamental to understanding the planet as a heat engine, and how Earth might be different from other planets and able to sustain life.\"</p><p>Krein's co-authors include Zachary Molitor, an EAPS graduate student, and Timothy Grove, the R.R. Schrock Professor of Geology at MIT.</p><h2>A chemical history</h2><p>The Earth's interior temperature has played a critical role in shaping the planet's surface over hundreds of millions of years. But there's been no way to directly read this temperature tens to hundreds of kilometers below the surface. Scientists have applied indirect means to infer the temperature of the upper mantle — the layer of the Earth just below the crust. But estimates thus far are inconclusive, and scientists disagree about how widely temperatures vary beneath the surface.</p><p>For their new study, Krein and her colleagues developed a new algorithm, called ReversePetrogen, that is designed to trace a rock's chemical history back in time, to identify its original composition of elements and determine the temperature at which the rock initially melted below the surface.</p><p>The algorithm is based on years of experiments carried out in Grove's lab to reproduce and characterize the melting processes of the Earth's interior. Researchers in the lab have heated up rocks of various compositions, reaching various temperatures and pressures, to observe their chemical evolution. From these experiments, the team has been able to derive equations — and ultimately, the new algorithm — to predict the relationships between a rock's temperature, pressure, and chemical composition.</p><p>Krein and her colleagues applied their new algorithm to rocks collected along the Earth's ocean ridges — a system of undersea volcanoes spanning more than 70,000 kilometers in length. Ocean ridges are regions where tectonic plates are spread apart by the eruption of material from the Earth's mantle — a process that is driven by underlying temperatures.</p><p>\"You could effectively make a model of the temperature of the entire interior of the Earth, based partly on the temperature at these ridges,\" Krein says. \"The question is, what is the data really telling us about the temperature variation in the mantle along the whole chain?\"</p><h2>Mantle map</h2><p>The data the team analyzed include more than 13,500 samples collected along the length of the ocean ridge system over several decades, by multiple research cruises. Each sample in the dataset is of an erupted sea glass — lava that erupted in the ocean and was instantly chilled by the surrounding water into a pristine, preserved form.</p><p>Scientists previously identified the chemical compositions of each glass in the dataset. Krein and her colleagues ran each sample's chemical compositions through their algorithm to determine the temperature at which each glass originally melted in the mantle.</p><p>In this way, the team was able to generate a map of mantle temperatures along the entire length of the ocean ridge system. From this map, they observed that much of the mantle is relatively homogenous, with an average temperature of around 1,350 degrees Celsius. There are however, \"hotspots,\" or regions along the ridge, where temperatures in the mantle appear significantly hotter, at around 1,600 degrees Celsius.</p><p>\"People think of hotspots as regions in the mantle where it's hotter, and where material may be melting more, and potentially rising faster, and we don't exactly know why, or how much hotter they are, or what the role of composition is at hotspots,\" Krein says. \"Some of these hotspots are on the ridge, and now we may get a sense of what the hotspot variation is globally using this new technique. That tells us something fundamental about the temperature of the Earth now, and now we can think of how it's changed over time.\"</p><p>Krein adds: \"Understanding these dynamics will help us better determine how continents grew and evolved on Earth, and when subduction and plate tectonics started — which are critical for complex life.\"</p><p>This research was supported, in part, by the National Science Foundation.</p><p>Reprinted with permission of <a href=\"https://news.mit.edu/\" target=\"_blank\">MIT News</a>. Read the <a href=\"https://news.mit.edu/2021/earth-inner-temperature-sea-glass-0729\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/RMreEzvxPGo\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 06 Aug 2021 06:57:11 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDg0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NTcwODExNn0.-zyzNImMW63E9O7IKsdLbsRM9oxnyVQu87xiH52TrrY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C1230%2C0%2C1032&height=700","linkMd5":"16ef425dd74111973ef716a17eeb74af","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn9@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-22-950_78a960f22df41b9e.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":101012,"destBytes":101012,"author":"Jennifer Chu | MIT News Office","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDg0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NTcwODExNn0.-zyzNImMW63E9O7IKsdLbsRM9oxnyVQu87xiH52TrrY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C1230%2C0%2C1032&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn9@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-22-950_78a960f22df41b9e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/RMreEzvxPGo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn52@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-27-983_43aae9e63dc7521b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588786},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Helmet worn at home shrank man's brain tumor by a third","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/glioblastoma-treatment-helmet-shrinks-tumor","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDgxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MzE5OTY4Nn0.V3b0DQOoTjv6FcWM6Fli8I1Be2_kTXNtCYodckjVOqw/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=608%2C0%2C458%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p><em>This <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/health/brain-tumor-treatment\" target=\"_blank\">article</a> was originally published on our sister site, Freethink.</em></p><p>A new brain tumor treatment appeared to <a href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fonc.2021.708017/full\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">shrink</a> a man's aggressive glioblastoma tumor by nearly a third — and all he had to do was wear a noninvasive helmet at home.</p><p><strong>The challenge:</strong> Glioblastoma is a rare but aggressive type of brain cancer that is <a href=\"https://www.thebraintumourcharity.org/brain-tumour-diagnosis-treatment/types-of-brain-tumour-adult/glioblastoma/glioblastoma-prognosis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">almost always fatal</a> in adults — 75% of patients die within a year of diagnosis, and only 5% live more than five years.</p><p>Treatment usually starts with risky surgery to remove the bulk of the brain tumor, after which a patient might undergo chemo or radiation therapy. </p><blockquote>\"Our results…open a new world of non-invasive and nontoxic therapy for brain cancer.\"<br/><cite>DAVID S. BASKIN</cite></blockquote><p>Not only can the side effects of those treatments hurt a patient's quality of life, but the treatments themselves can't actually <em>cure</em> the <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/health/urine-test-for-brain-cancer\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">brain cancer</a> — they just buy the patient a little more time.</p><p><strong>Why it matters: </strong><a href=\"https://braintumor.org/take-action/about-gbm/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Survival rates</a> for glioblastoma have remained mostly stagnant over the past few decades, meaning our ability to treat the deadly <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/health/ebola-to-fight-brain-cancer\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">brain cancer</a> isn't getting much better.</p><p>If that doesn't change, we'll continue to lose about <a href=\"https://www.gliocure.com/en/patients/glioblastoma/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">200,000 people</a> to the disease every year, worldwide.</p><p><strong>New </strong><strong>brain tumor treatment</strong><strong>: </strong>In a past study, researchers at Houston Methodist Neurological Institute found they could kill glioblastoma cells in the lab by subjecting them to oscillating magnetic fields, which they created by using electricity to rotate magnets in a precise way.</p><p>They believe the fields disrupt the transportation of electrons during the process used to create energy for cells. However, compounds produced by tumor cells are needed to trigger this disruption, meaning healthy cells should be spared while glioblastoma cells die.</p><p><strong>The case study: </strong>In 2019, the researchers received approval under the FDA's compassionate use protocol to test the therapy on a man whose brain tumor wasn't responding to aggressive <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/health/new-cancer-treatment-cbd\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">cancer treatments</a>.</p><blockquote>\"Imagine treating brain cancer without radiation therapy or chemotherapy.\"<br/><cite>DAVID S. BASKIN</cite></blockquote><p>Over the course of three days, they trained the man and his wife how to deliver the therapy using a helmet equipped with three rotating magnets.</p><p>They then sent him home with the helmet and instructions to administer the brain tumor treatment for two hours every day at first and then work his way up to six hours.</p><p><strong>The results: </strong>The man used the helmet for 36 days before suffering an unrelated head injury that led to his death. His family gave the researchers permission to autopsy his brain, and they found that his tumor had shrunk by 31% since the start of study. </p><p>\"Thanks to the courage of this patient and his family, we were able to test and verify the potential effectiveness of the first noninvasive therapy for glioblastoma in the world,\" corresponding author David S. Baskin said in a <a href=\"https://www.houstonmethodist.org/newsroom/investigational-magnetic-device-shrinks-glioblastoma-in-first-in-world-human-test/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">press release</a>.</p><p><strong>Looking ahead: </strong>While this study is encouraging, the researchers will need to prove their brain tumor treatment can help more than a single patient.</p><p>The unlucky head injury also means we don't know if shrinking the tumor in the short-run improves survival rates. But if it can, the helmet could mark a turning point in the battle against glioblastoma.</p><p>\"Imagine treating brain cancer without radiation therapy or chemotherapy,\" Baskin said. \"Our results in the laboratory and with this patient open a new world of non-invasive and nontoxic therapy for brain cancer, with many exciting possibilities for the future.\"</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/9hlGZtiaAEw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sun, 08 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDgxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MzE5OTY4Nn0.V3b0DQOoTjv6FcWM6Fli8I1Be2_kTXNtCYodckjVOqw/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=608%2C0%2C458%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"662529a808b7ff04a952b0eba482841e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn23@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-25-050_cc5d54e2abe88ac2.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":22024,"destBytes":22024,"author":"Kristin Houser","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDgxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MzE5OTY4Nn0.V3b0DQOoTjv6FcWM6Fli8I1Be2_kTXNtCYodckjVOqw/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=608%2C0%2C458%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn23@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-25-050_cc5d54e2abe88ac2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/9hlGZtiaAEw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn47@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-092_171e3eadb4a7f1d5.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588784},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Social physics: Are we at a tipping point in world history?","link":"https://bigthink.com/13-8/tipping-point-history-social-physics","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4Nzg1OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTk3MzEzNH0.KcfAd_Jgstkyht_GnvSP0qt0SrNSF9mCqLXy0guNoqs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>This moment in history feels different, as if we really are on the cusp of something epoch-making for good or ill. Does that mean we're at a tipping point?</li><li>If we are, then we can ask two questions: (1) Does history have a grand narrative? (2) Is there an arc to history?</li><li>Social physics, which is a branch of complex system theory, can help answer these questions.</li></ul><hr/><p>This moment in history certainly feels different. The assumptions built into the Cold War and post-Cold War political order that I grew up with are evaporating. Digital technologies are pushing us into entirely new and unknown social forms at speeds that make even the last century's change seem languid. And, most importantly, the planet's climate is changing rapidly in ways that are likely to push hard on our global project of civilization. Looking at all these movements of politics, culture, and even the planet itself, it is hard to know if there are greater forces at work or whether it is all just a random mess of chaos. </p><p>In other words, is there an arc to history, or is it just a random walk to no place in particular?</p><p>Now, I am just a simple country astrophysicist and not a historian, so my perspective on this question skews in two directions. First, I do think (and write) a lot on the history of science and technology. I am particularly interested in the way science and culture have been braided over the last five centuries to create this high-tech, energy-intensive version of human civilization. Second, a new and fascinating transdisciplinary field of social physics is emerging that combines history, statistical mechanics, and data science to look for deeper (and even predictive) patterns during human events.</p><p>So, beginning from these two perspectives, let's ask the question again in two different ways: (1) Does history have a grand narrative? (2) Is there an arc to history?</p><h2>Does history have a grand narrative?</h2><p>A grand narrative is a constellation of ideas and stories that attempts to make sense of a particular moment in history. The Enlightenment is an example of a grand narrative. Beginning in the mid 1700s, people across Europe began assembling a set of new ideas about a society based on reason and equality. They were rejecting the old feudal social and religious orders and seeking to replace them with something they imagined would be better. Science played an essential part in this conception of \"better.\" The already stunning progress of science served as a model that Enlightenment thinkers believed could be used to progress toward something more just, free, and equitable. In this way, the Enlightenment thinkers believed there was a grand narrative for their moment in history, and by fleshing it out in ideas, they provided a kind of mental blueprint for what followed. </p><blockquote> Looking at all these movements of politics, culture, and even the planet itself, it is hard to know if there are greater forces at work or whether it is all just a random mess of chaos.</blockquote><p>But was that blueprint really followed? Or is it just in 20/20 hindsight that we pull something out of the randomness of events and call it \"The Enlightenment\"? It is, after all, easy to see how random events can shape history. There is the old ditty about how \"<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/For_Want_of_a_Nail\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">for the want of a shoe the horse was lost</a>.\" That random event leads all the way to the loss of a kingdom. It is hard to deny that randomness and chaos also play a part in the course of events.</p><h2>Is there an arc to history?</h2><p>So, let's ask the second version of our question: Is there an arc to history? </p><p>This is where <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-019-53300-4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">social physics</a> comes in. Social physics is really just a branch of complex system theory (which is a field you can expect a lot more posts on because it is currently blowing my mind). Complex systems embrace everything from cells to microbial colonies to ecosystems to financial systems to human societies. It is truly a transdisciplinary field that uses insights from all kinds of domains to build something entirely new. One of those insights is the recognition of how randomness can play a central role in pushing a system from one \"state\" to another. </p><p>Complex systems are, well… complex. There can be many variables or degrees of freedom that comprise them. For a social order, it may be the level of economic output, income inequality, literacy, life expectancy, and so forth. What is amazing about complex systems is that, in spite of this complexity, they can settle into stable configurations for long periods. You may have kings and lords and clergy and serfs for 1000 years or more. During that time, random events are always happening. Things like bad harvests occur, and they may lead to bread riots in the villages. But somehow those random events do not perturb the system's stability.</p><h2>Tipping points</h2><p>But then there are moments in the system's evolution when it begins nearing what are called \"critical points\" or \"tipping points.\" The background dynamic of the system is ripe for being blown up. It is only at a critical point that a random bread riot can suddenly escalate into a storming of the palace, which then leads to an overturn of the ruling council, which then possibly leads to an entirely new social arrangement.</p><p>For me at least, complex systems help us understand how the two forms of the question about grand narratives get put together. The arc of history is not smooth, and it is not determined beforehand. Randomness as a social system approaches critical points is all important. But grand narratives spun by writers, philosophers, artists, politicians, and scientists can create a background of ideas. As you near a tipping point, those ideas can get picked up and amplified to become the organizing principles for the new arrangement that emerges.</p><p>So, the big questions now become: (1) Are we near a tipping point? (2) What are the new ideas that stand ready to take us someplace new and better?</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/370GrPBz4Rs\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4Nzg1OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTk3MzEzNH0.KcfAd_Jgstkyht_GnvSP0qt0SrNSF9mCqLXy0guNoqs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"5f3cc7ef19290c910f1683416bf84f57","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn36@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-11-935_5bd9b9c8299031b0.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":15972,"destBytes":15972,"author":"Adam Frank","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4Nzg1OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNTk3MzEzNH0.KcfAd_Jgstkyht_GnvSP0qt0SrNSF9mCqLXy0guNoqs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn36@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-11-935_5bd9b9c8299031b0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/370GrPBz4Rs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn5@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-27-987_75d4aafdc46daf4a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588781},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Three powerful technologies we borrowed from nature","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/technology-borrowed-from-nature","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MzQyNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MzY3NTE5Nn0.czPe1DJ3XqyTIVuKgYIL1jVvvRhWBzgt1BbnjLL3KVg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p>\n\t<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/scitable/topicpage/discovery-of-dna-structure-and-function-watson-397/\" target=\"_blank\">Watson and Crick</a>, <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/physics/1933/schrodinger/biographical/\" target=\"_blank\">Schrödinger</a> and <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/physics/1921/einstein/biographical/\" target=\"_blank\">Einstein</a> all made theoretical breakthroughs that have changed the world's understanding of science.\n</p><hr/><p>\n\tToday big, game-changing ideas are less common. New and improved techniques are the <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth1004-1\" target=\"_blank\">driving force behind modern scientific research and discoveries</a>. They allow scientists – <a href=\"https://scholar.google.com/citations?user=RpiSPiwAAAAJ&hl=en&oi=ao\" target=\"_blank\">including chemists like me</a> – to do our experiments faster than before, and they shine light on areas of science hidden to our predecessors.\n</p><p>\n\tThree cutting-edge techniques – the gene-editing tool <a href=\"https://www.newscientist.com/definition/what-is-crispr/\" target=\"_blank\">CRISPR</a>, <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1242/jcs.072744\" target=\"_blank\">fluorescent proteins</a> and <a href=\"https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/optogenetics-controlling/\" target=\"_blank\">optogenetics</a> – were all inspired by nature. Biomolecular tools that have worked for bacteria, jellyfish and algae for millions of years are now being used in medicine and biological research. Directly or indirectly, they will change the lives of everyday people.\n</p><h2>Bacterial defense systems as genetic editors</h2><p>\n\tBacteria and viruses battle themselves and one another. They are at constant biochemical war, <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cub.2019.04.024\" target=\"_blank\">competing for scarce resources</a>.\n</p><p>\n\tOne of the weapons that bacteria have in their arsenal is the <a href=\"https://www.livescience.com/58790-crispr-explained.html\" target=\"_blank\">CRISPR-Cas system</a>. It is a genetic library consisting of short repeats of DNA gathered over time from hostile viruses, paired with a protein called Cas that can cut viral DNA as if with scissors. In the natural world, when bacteria are attacked by viruses whose DNA has been stored in the CRISPR archive, the CRISPR-Cas system hunts down, cuts and destroys the viral DNA.\n</p><p>\n\tScientists have repurposed these weapons for their own use, with groundbreaking effect. Jennifer Doudna, a biochemist based at the University of California, Berkeley, and French microbiologist Emmanuelle Charpentier shared the <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/nobel-prize-for-chemistry-honors-exquisitely-precise-gene-editing-technique-crispr-a-gene-engineer-explains-how-it-works-147701\" target=\"_blank\">2020 Nobel Prize in chemistry</a> for <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/chemistry/2020/doudna/lecture/\" target=\"_blank\">the development of</a> <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/nobel-prize-for-crispr-honors-two-great-scientists-and-leaves-out-many-others-147730\" target=\"_blank\">CRISPR-Cas as a gene-editing technique</a>.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img alt=\"University of California, Berkeley Professor of Chemistry Jennifer A. Doudna and Ulmea Universtiy Proffessor and Microbologist Emmanuelle Charpentier.\" class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"ef0747e5ef3fdd273c98257159f0de82\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"ac38a\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY0NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjQ2NzAwM30.QZ3HstAYAYU_bxJZ90swCLyqKRKvVt1BHx2DlYySx8Q/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C284&height=700\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Photo by Steve Jennings/Getty Images for Breakthrough Prize</small></p>\n        <p>The <a href=\"https://www.genome.gov/human-genome-project\" target=\"_blank\">Human Genome Project</a> has provided a nearly complete genetic sequence for humans and given scientists a template to sequence all other organisms. However, before CRISPR-Cas, we researchers didn't have the tools to easily access and edit the genes in living organisms. Today, thanks to CRISPR-Cas, lab work that used to take months and years and cost hundreds of thousands of dollars can be done in less than a week for just a few hundred dollars.<br/></p><p>\n\tThere are more than 10,000 genetic disorders caused by mutations that occur on only one gene, the <a href=\"http://hihg.med.miami.edu/thromboticstorm/genetics-overview/single-gene-disorders\">so-called single-gene disorders</a>. They affect millions of people. <a href=\"https://www.genome.gov/Genetic-Disorders/Sickle-Cell-Disease\" target=\"_blank\">Sickle cell anemia</a>, <a href=\"https://www.cff.org/What-is-CF/Genetics/CF-Genetics-The-Basics/\" target=\"_blank\">cystic fibrosis</a> and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.31887/DCNS.2016.18.1/pnopoulos\" target=\"_blank\">Huntington's disease</a> are among the most well-known of these disorders. These are all obvious targets for CRISPR therapy because it is much simpler to fix or replace just one defective gene rather than needing to correct errors on multiple genes.\n</p><p>\n\tFor example, in preclinical studies, <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1056/NEJMoa2107454\" target=\"_blank\">researchers injected</a> an encapsuled CRISPR system into patients born with a rare genetic disease, <a href=\"https://rarediseases.info.nih.gov/diseases/656/familial-transthyretin-amyloidosis\" target=\"_blank\">transthyretin amyloidosis</a>, that causes fatal nerve and heart conditions. Preliminary results from the study demonstrated <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/d41586-021-01776-4\" target=\"_blank\">that CRISPR-Cas can be injected</a> directly into patients in such a way that it can find and edit the faulty genes associated with a disease. In the six patients included in this landmark work, the encapsuled CRISPR-Cas minimissiles reached their target genes and did their job, causing a significant drop in a <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/scitable/topicpage/protein-misfolding-and-degenerative-diseases-14434929/\" target=\"_blank\">misfolded protein</a> associated with the disease.\n</p><h2>Jellyfish light up the microscopic world</h2><p>\n\tThe <a href=\"https://faculty.washington.edu/cemills/Aequorea.html\" target=\"_blank\">crystal jellyfish, Aequorea victoria</a>, which drifts aimlessly in the northern Pacific, has no brain, no anus and no poisonous stingers. It is an unlikely candidate to ignite a revolution in biotechnology. Yet on the periphery of its umbrella, it has about 300 photo-organs that give off pinpricks of green light that have changed the way science is conducted.\n</p><p>\n\tThis bioluminescent light in the jellyfish stems from a luminescent protein called aequorin and a fluorescent molecule called <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1242/jcs.072744\" target=\"_blank\">green fluorescent protein</a>, or GFP. In modern biotechnology GFP acts as a molecular lightbulb that can be fused to other proteins, allowing researchers to track them and to see when and where proteins are being made in the cells of living organisms. Fluorescent protein technology is used in thousands of labs every day and has resulted in the awarding of two Nobel Prizes, <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/chemistry/2008/popular-information/\" target=\"_blank\">one in 2008</a> and the <a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/chemistry/2014/summary/\" target=\"_blank\">other in 2014</a>. And fluorescent proteins have now been found in <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1242/jcs.072744\" target=\"_blank\">many more species</a>.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img alt=\"Fluorescence from fluorescent proteins excited with UV light. Picture taken by Erik A. Rodriguez.\" class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"1be8931c64002c6c625a14272921c047\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"e40f9\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY0My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4Njc0MzczMn0.SxG-lta8qAZo2FEluvWZHSsWyDbsu6wdLWMfSAlYZYg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C39%2C0%2C39&height=700\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=User:Erin_Rod&action=edit&redlink=1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"User:Erin Rod (page does not exist)\">Erin Rod</a> via Wikimedia Commons.</small></p>\n        <p>This technology proved its utility once again when researchers created genetically modified <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2020.05.042\" target=\"_blank\">COVID-19 viruses that express GFP</a>. The resulting fluorescence makes it possible to follow the path of the viruses as they enter the respiratory system and bind to surface cells with hairlike structures.<br/></p><h2>Algae let us play the brain neuron by neuron</h2><p>\n\tWhen algae, which depend on sunlight for growth, are placed in a large aquarium in a darkened room, they swim around aimlessly. But if a lamp is turned on, the algae will swim toward the light. The single-celled <a href=\"https://www.britannica.com/science/flagellate\" target=\"_blank\">flagellates</a> – so named for the whiplike appendages they use to move around – don't have eyes. Instead, they have a structure called an eyespot that distinguishes between light and darkness. The eyespot is studded with <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1525538113\" target=\"_blank\">light-sensitive proteins called channelrhodopsins</a>.\n</p><p>\n\tIn the early 2000s, <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/nn1525\" target=\"_blank\">researchers discovered</a> that when they genetically inserted these channelrhodopsins into the nerve cells of any organism, illuminating the channelrhodopsins with blue light caused neurons to fire. This technique, known as optogenetics, involves inserting the algae gene that makes channelrhodopsin into neurons. When a pinpoint beam of blue light is shined on these neurons, the channelrhodopsins open up, calcium ions flood through the neurons and the neurons fire.\n</p><p>\n\tUsing this tool, scientists can stimulate groups of neurons selectively and repeatedly, thereby gaining a more precise understanding of which neurons to target to treat specific disorders and diseases. Optogenetics might hold the key to treating debilitating and deadly brain diseases, such as Alzheimer's and Parkinson's.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img alt=\"Image of a glowing human brain model.\" class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"3b27e10d238f634f8b8783fe1b33ee03\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"d3c3b\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjYyMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NzM3OTQ0Nn0.IuH77TMEZMjajCI1gZYH1cX5EyFg1ISVUCZAwE-xuCk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C377%2C0%2C378&height=700\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Photo by <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/@natcon773?utm_source=unsplash&utm_medium=referral&utm_content=creditCopyText\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Natasha Connell</a> on Unsplash</small></p>\n        <p>But optogenetics isn't only useful for understanding the brain. Researchers have used optogenetic techniques <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1038/s41591-021-01351-4\" target=\"_blank\">to partially reverse blindness</a> and have found promising results in clinical trials using optogenetics on patients with <a href=\"https://www.nei.nih.gov/learn-about-eye-health/eye-conditions-and-diseases/retinitis-pigmentosa\" target=\"_blank\">retinitis pigmentosa</a>, a group of genetic disorders that break down retinal cells. And in mouse studies, the technique has been used to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pbiomolbio.2019.08.013\" target=\"_blank\">manipulate heartbeat</a> and <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1016/j.autneu.2020.102733\" target=\"_blank\">regulate bowel movements of constipated mice</a>.<br/></p><h2>What else lies within nature's toolbox?</h2><p>\n\tWhat undiscovered techniques does nature still hold for us?\n</p><p>\n\tAccording to <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1711842115\" target=\"_blank\">a 2018 study</a>, people represent just 0.01% of all living things by mass but have caused the loss of 83% of all wild mammals and half of all plants in our brief time on Earth. By annihilating nature, humankind might be losing out on new, powerful and life-altering techniques without having even imagined them.\n</p><p>\n\tAfter all, no one could have foreseen that the discovery of three groundbreaking processes derived from nature could change the way science is done.<img alt=\"The Conversation\" height=\"1\" src=\"https://counter.theconversation.com/content/164459/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic\" width=\"1\"/>\n</p><p>\n\t<a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/marc-zimmer-160444\" target=\"_blank\">Marc Zimmer</a>, Professor of Chemistry, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/connecticut-college-1921\" target=\"_blank\">Connecticut College</a></em>\n</p><p>\n\tThis article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\" target=\"_blank\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. article</a>.\n</p><p><img alt=\"The Conversation\" height=\"1\" src=\"https://counter.theconversation.com/content/164459/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-advanced\" width=\"1\"/></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/a--KxHefiho\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Aug 2021 15:19:52 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MzQyNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MzY3NTE5Nn0.czPe1DJ3XqyTIVuKgYIL1jVvvRhWBzgt1BbnjLL3KVg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&height=700","linkMd5":"4e3a07eeff8e9b42df0f2e0458904a73","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn16@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-11-924_f110b748e5b04547.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":43880,"destBytes":43880,"author":"Marc Zimmer","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MzQyNi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0MzY3NTE5Nn0.czPe1DJ3XqyTIVuKgYIL1jVvvRhWBzgt1BbnjLL3KVg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C312%2C0%2C312&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn16@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-11-924_f110b748e5b04547.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY0NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNjQ2NzAwM30.QZ3HstAYAYU_bxJZ90swCLyqKRKvVt1BHx2DlYySx8Q/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C284&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn9@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-113_ac38af94c69d2d8c.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY0My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4Njc0MzczMn0.SxG-lta8qAZo2FEluvWZHSsWyDbsu6wdLWMfSAlYZYg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C39%2C0%2C39&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn64@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-086_e40f965ff13b49a9.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjYyMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NzM3OTQ0Nn0.IuH77TMEZMjajCI1gZYH1cX5EyFg1ISVUCZAwE-xuCk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C377%2C0%2C378&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn27@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-110_d3c3ba6f2fe3421f.webp","https://counter.theconversation.com/content/164459/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-basic":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn31@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-28-000_d8f06b9c83ab7de7.webp","https://counter.theconversation.com/content/164459/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-advanced":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn56@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-27-970_91e082d400faa5a3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/a--KxHefiho":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn71@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-146_ae1949270ad76bed.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Wandering icebergs could trigger tsunamis","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/iceberg-marine-landslide","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ2Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MTg0NjMyMH0.phvRqR66x3yoOpv4KnDSW0zz9kNKmc_z4F77IiPrDa8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Earthquakes can cause landslides on the ocean floor, which in turn can trigger tsunamis. </li><li>A new study finds that wandering icebergs can produce submarine landslides, too, when they hit ground and capsize.</li><li>The increasing number of icebergs wandering the warming oceans poses a previously under-appreciated geohazard.</li></ul><p>Last May, a chunk of ice destined to become the world's largest iceberg broke free of the western side of Antarctica's <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filchner%E2%80%93Ronne_Ice_Shelf\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Filchner-Ronne Ice Shelf</a>. Bigger than Rhode Island, the 105-mile long, 15-mile wide, 1667-square-mile iceberg — now<a href=\"https://www.livescience.com/biggest-iceberg-breaks-free.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> named A-76</a> — slid into the Weddell Sea. The loss of significant chunks of Antarctic ice is happening with alarming regularity as the planet warms.</p><p>Icebergs can be deep enough to scrape the sea floor. They also can be large enough to cause tsunamis as they splash into the sea, and the huge volumes of water they displace can threaten ships and damage marine structures such as platforms and undersea internet cabling.</p><p>Now, a<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41561-021-00767-4\" target=\"_blank\"> new study</a> reveals that smaller icebergs, chunks whose keel does not even touch bottom, can produce underwater landslides when they hit a shoreline and capsize, possibly <a href=\"https://www.usgs.gov/faqs/how-do-landslides-cause-tsunamis?qt-news_science_products=0#qt-news_science_products\" target=\"_blank\">resulting in a tsunami</a>. This poses a previously under-appreciated geohazard.</p><p><span></span>The study notes, \"Icebergs originating from the Arctic, Greenland and Antarctica are hazards thousands of kilometers away from their original source and can affect continental slopes by triggering submarine landslides.\"</p><h2>The iceberg cometh</h2>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"306606a83e4c03fdd8e7273aa1e98d96\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"12800\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ5My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjI2MTc1NH0.qghtxBzOL1dBavAFJnmS7WM6ofA-gjipbVg5wkqPrLQ/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">A Baffin island fjord</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit:<a href=\"https://www.flickr.com/photos/dihade/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> MARINEBUGS</a> / Flickr</small></p>\n        <p>The worrying insight is the product of some detective work that revealed an underwater landslide in the Southwind Fjord off of Nunavut near Canada's<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baffin_Island\" target=\"_blank\"> Baffin Island</a>, some time between September 2018 and September 2019.<br/></p><p>Marine landslides are notoriously hard to analyze since they typically occur out of sight. For the study, the researchers consulted satellite data, seafloor bathymetric data, and analyses of the local seabed's composition to solve a geological whodunnit. </p><p>The telltale signs of the landslide were found in a comparison of high-resolution, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multibeam_echosounder\" target=\"_blank\">multibeam echosounder</a> data of the fjord's seabed captured in 2013, 2014, 2018, and 2019. The earliest depicted a gently sloping, \"featureless\" seabed. By the later readings, however, two smaller<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scarp\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> scarps</a> had appeared. </p><p>The story told is of a primary landslide some time between August 27, 2018 ( the date of the 2018 readings) and September 24, 2019 (the date of the next bathymetric survey). There were also signs of two adjacent landslides in the same time period, which occurred as the two smaller scarps failed. There were no reports of large earthquakes within 300 kilometers of the fjord during this period.</p><p>However, there was the geological equivalent of a smoking gun: the scientists observed 20-27-meter-deep iceberg pits, depressions indicative of an iceberg impact.</p><h2>Caught on camera</h2>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"eb55f79436d04c5fda97681ec71d9042\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"cbb7c\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ5Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTEzNDY5OH0.8vAUKledWhEYSheH8yoZzPEMn3FXnZPmgdSGyl8bXgs/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Sentinel-2</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit:<a href=\"https://www.esa.int/Enabling_Support/Operations/Sentinel-2_operations\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> ESA/ATG Medialab</a></small></p>\n        <p>Imagery from the<a href=\"https://sentinel.esa.int/web/sentinel/missions/sentinel-2\" target=\"_blank\"> Sentinel 2 satellite</a> revealed the perpetrator as it floated into the fjord around August 27, 2018, became grounded between September 1-4, and was no longer intact and visible by early October. The interloper's location lined up perfectly with the seabed data — the iceberg grounded 3 meters upslope of the ice pits in the first few days of September 2018 and wound up 3 meters downslope from them by the 9th. The narrative suggested is that between September 4 and 9, the iceberg capsized, hit the seafloor, and broke into two pieces.<br/></p><h2>Not just a threat to unsinkable ships</h2><p>The study exposes a new marine geohazard, as it was previously assumed that submarine landslides were caused only by earthquakes. It was also believed that iceberg damage was constrained to continental shelves and upper slopes as icebergs floated into water that is too shallow for their depth.</p><p>Now, however, the study concludes: \"These results indicate that icebergs grounding and capsizing may be responsible for triggering submarine landslides in many fjords and on continental slopes in polar to sub-polar environments, representing a previously underestimated hazard.\"</p><p>Coastal cities, beware the wandering iceberg.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/YTnFuqZrw4k\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 09 Aug 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ2Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MTg0NjMyMH0.phvRqR66x3yoOpv4KnDSW0zz9kNKmc_z4F77IiPrDa8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"a15438b2f2a4ba9e079cd6e4379f530e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn49@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-192_e28a795e88aa1fd7.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":191722,"destBytes":191722,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ2Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MTg0NjMyMH0.phvRqR66x3yoOpv4KnDSW0zz9kNKmc_z4F77IiPrDa8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn49@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-192_e28a795e88aa1fd7.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ5My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjI2MTc1NH0.qghtxBzOL1dBavAFJnmS7WM6ofA-gjipbVg5wkqPrLQ/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn98@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-109_1280089e2707903f.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTQ5Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTEzNDY5OH0.8vAUKledWhEYSheH8yoZzPEMn3FXnZPmgdSGyl8bXgs/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn88@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-28-256_cbb7c57174a88f7f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/YTnFuqZrw4k":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn76@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-419_507e7cee52ddb34a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588783},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Beauty bias: hopefully the judge finds you attractive","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/beauty-bias-justice","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NzI3ODA4N30.jtHLPpdWSVg5CN1WoWPwioYIc0oix51m2uUrLjxpsAk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C2%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Despite our attempts to be unbiased, people often judge others based on looks alone. </li><li>In the courtroom, physically attractive defendants often get lighter sentences than others. </li><li>We unconsciously assume that attractive people are as great as they look.</li></ul><hr/><p>Try as we might, humans often struggle to be the rational, unbiased creatures that we aspire to be. We routinely fall victim to cognitive biases, systemic errors in our thinking, and the glitches in our institutions that perpetuate them. </p><p>One such case is the repeated finding that attractive people and unattractive people often get different treatment in courts of law.</p><h2>Justice is not blind to beauty</h2><p>A large study examining crimes tried in courts noted that fines and bail levels tended to be greater for defendants rated as less attractive by<a href=\"https://psycnet.apa.org/record/1992-11218-001\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>observers</u></a>. Another older<a href=\"https://psycnet.apa.org/record/1981-05341-001\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>study</u></a> produced similar results for a variety of sentences, with better looking people serving less time. (However, the study did not find that they were convicted less overall.)</p><p>A number of studies using simulated juries suggests that this bias exists even when the crimes in question are not real. One simulation found that test subjects gave longer sentences to uglier people than to attractive ones for the same fictitious<a href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.2466/pr0.1997.81.2.679\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>crime</u></a> when given pictures to look at. Another found that test subjects were less certain of the guilt of attractive<a href=\"https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/0092656674900440?via%3Dihub\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>people</u></a>.</p><p>Some of the studies into this phenomenon find similar effects for other non-relevant details about those accused of crimes. One found that smiling does wonders for people accused of crimes: they tend to get lower sentences than otherwise<a href=\"https://www-proquest-com.ezproxy.depaul.edu/docview/199834355?OpenUrlRefId=info:xri/sid:primo&accountid=10477\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>expected</u></a>. Another found that the expression of remorse — which is often independent of the feeling — can seriously impact how a jury decides to act.</p><p>Because there is nothing new under the sun, this is not limited to modern America either. The ancient Greek story of Phryne the courtesan tells us that a Greek jury decided against finding her guilty of impiety on the grounds that a woman so beautiful must be in the good graces of the gods.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image image-crop-16x9\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"2f165596eef26794890ddd1d6897e301\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"87a6f\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjIxNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNDUyOTEwNX0.VaVn6gaPNx-PwK7t1TVVidmkO6tjXOmaHHDkKTh8CJw/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C109%2C0%2C-1&height=700\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\"><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phryne_before_the_Areopagus\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\" title=\"Phryne before the Areopagus\">Phryne before the Areopagus</a></small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">By Jean-Léon Gérôme - Uploaded by Popszes on 19 February 2006., Public Domain, <a href=\"https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?curid=586161\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?curid=58...</a></small></p><h2>The good, the bad, and the ugly</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/BzG0_aU_Fn0\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>In general, we seem to associate attractiveness with virtue. This \"attractiveness bias\" causes people to assume that more attractive people are smarter, saner, more sociable, and more<a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4757567/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>competent</u></a> than the ugly. Even babies seem to have this bias, being much more willing to pay attention to good looking people than others.</p><p>Studies also suggest — and this is not much of a surprise — that the attractive are more popular, make more money, and report less anxiety than others. The causes of these outcomes remain up for debate, though one can suppose that the aforementioned stereotypes likely make it easier for them to make friends and secure good jobs. The existence of this bias in hiring is well known and is considered a problem in some circles.</p><p>An interdisciplinary<a href=\"https://www.proquest.com/openview/70e45e152aa065eeca264a069ea47181/1?pq-origsite=gscholar&cbl=47829\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>take</u></a> on why we have this bias suggests that the explanation may be fairly simple: people act nicely to attractive people because we are attracted to them. They argue that the shift in behavior many people have in the presence of beautiful individuals is in line with the predictions of evolutionary psychology.</p><h2>Is there a way to correct this bias?</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/8kscrS-_C7s\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>Some studies suggest that the courtroom manifestation of the bias can be overcome by jurors and test subjects spending more time considering the<a href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.2466/pr0.102.3.727-733\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>problem</u></a> of guilt, though others suggest this method may be inconsistent.</p><p>The beauty bias pops up everywhere. When it comes to hiring, several authorities suggest taking a standardized approach to dealing with people to assure that meaningful things, like qualifications and evidence, are highlighted while trivialities, like looks, are<a href=\"https://www.shrm.org/resourcesandtools/hr-topics/talent-acquisition/pages/how-to-avoid-beauty-bias-when-hiring.aspx\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>downplayed</u></a>.</p><p>Humans unconsciously assume that good looking people are as great as they look. This bias is deeply ingrained and unlikely just to vanish. If you have a court date anytime soon, go dressed in your best attire. And if you are guilty, at least pretend to be sorry.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/KzSnhoKZxyE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Aug 2021 15:00:01 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NzI3ODA4N30.jtHLPpdWSVg5CN1WoWPwioYIc0oix51m2uUrLjxpsAk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C2%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"8b65ba616ec8319537323f968de3a816","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn55@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-22-976_653cd6e3cb060b75.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":106094,"destBytes":106094,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQxMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NzI3ODA4N30.jtHLPpdWSVg5CN1WoWPwioYIc0oix51m2uUrLjxpsAk/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C2%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn55@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-22-976_653cd6e3cb060b75.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjIxNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYzNDUyOTEwNX0.VaVn6gaPNx-PwK7t1TVVidmkO6tjXOmaHHDkKTh8CJw/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C109%2C0%2C-1&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn15@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-084_87a6fd55ee5c606a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/KzSnhoKZxyE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn33@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-984_ab6f92247a3f219c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15
