space","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/nasa-lucy-mission-time-capsule","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyNjQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4ODUxNzM4OH0.9YlNp1cWI5iyBbOFqPmxfPBnwLCVY7aHvYmZp3UHJQs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C4%2C0%2C131&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p><em>This <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/space/time-capsule\" target=\"_blank\">article</a> was originally published by our sister site, Freethink.</em></p><p>Ever since my fifth-grade class buried a jar of notes in the playground, time capsules have captured my imagination. We intended to unearth it when we graduated high school, but that never happened, and the note I wrote on that pink index card remains a mystery, even to me.</p><p>NASA has also been fascinated by time and the future. In the '70s, NASA's first four spacecraft to leave our solar system had time capsules — plaques inscribed with messages to possible alien life. </p><blockquote>NASA sent the time capsule to space hoping that astro-archeologists of the future will find it and learn a bit about early space exploration and life today.<br/></blockquote><p>Once again, NASA is sending a message to space. This time, the message is meant for future generations of humankind. </p><p><strong>Lucy in the sky with diamonds: </strong>The latest time capsule will be carried aboard the NASA spacecraft, Lucy, bound for the Trojan asteroids. The cluster of rocks shares an orbit with Jupiter, circling the sun. </p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube\">\n        <span class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d7df68c9237a421e56e3f80c7354f9d7\" style=\"display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;\"><iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"auto\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/RAJ9ORwf0oM?rel=0\" style=\"position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></span>\n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">NASA Lucy Mission's Message to the Future</small>\n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">\n            <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=RAJ9ORwf0oM\" target=\"_blank\">www.youtube.com</a>\n        </small>\n        </p>\n        <p>Because the rocks are like space fossils, NASA named the spacecraft after Lucy, the fossilized human ancestor discovered in 1974.<br/></p><p>In 2033, Lucy will visit eight asteroids that are on six independent orbits around the sun. When Lucy finishes, it will continue to orbit among the Trojan asteroids for thousands or possibly even millions of years, NASA <a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/2021/nasa-lucy-mission-s-message-to-the-future\" target=\"_blank\">reports</a> — long enough for future generations of human explorers to rediscover after it has been forgotten. </p><blockquote>Albert Einstein, poet Joy Harjo, Martin Luther King Jr., Yoko Ono, Carl Sagan, and The Beatles all make an appearance on the golden plaque.<br/></blockquote><p><strong>What is the message?</strong> NASA sent the time capsule to space hoping that astro-archeologists of the future will find it and learn a bit about early space exploration and life today. They filled the plaque with messages from prominent members of society — words of advice and wisdom to our descendants in the future. </p><p>Quotes by Nobel Laureates in Literature and United States Poet Laureates were among the messages to future generations. People like Albert Einstein, poet Joy Harjo, Martin Luther King Jr., Yoko Ono, Carl Sagan, and The Beatles all make an appearance on the golden plaque. Even Amanda Gorman, known for the poem she read at President Joe Biden's inauguration, contributed to the plaque. </p><p>Some of the messages include: </p><p>\"There are no curses – only mirrors held up to the souls of gods and mortals,\" by Rita Dove, the first African American Poet Laureate.</p><p>\"Who Wants to live Forever? – if love must die,\" by singer-songwriter and astrophysicist Brian May.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"343551d85b035d61f76516d3689b7602\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"571ed\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyNjQ2MS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NDQ1NDIxM30.STzdlLm-VL7AuA95JcnxRc3YfHFQQndyqyQkDjohu9g/img.png?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: NASA</small></p>\n        <p>You can read the entire message on NASA's Lucy Plaque <a href=\"http://lucy.swri.edu/LucyPlaque.html\" target=\"_blank\">website</a>. The plaque also includes a current picture of the Solar System and a map of Lucy's trajectory.<br/></p><p>We earthlings are constantly creating a <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/culture/pandemic-time-capsule\" target=\"_blank\">time capsule</a> for future generations. Libraries, the internet, and even Freethink.com are an archive of messages to ourselves about our collective values, struggles, and accomplishments. One day we'll look back on the fantastic innovations of today — like <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/technology/mcdonalds-ai-drive-thru-attendants\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">artificial intelligence</a>, <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/technology/chinas-nuclear-fusion-reactor\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">nuclear fusion</a>, or a <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/environment/floating-city\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">floating city</a> — and reminisce at how simple they seem. </p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/ZUXn6bmwhw0\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 14 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyNjQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4ODUxNzM4OH0.9YlNp1cWI5iyBbOFqPmxfPBnwLCVY7aHvYmZp3UHJQs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C4%2C0%2C131&height=700","linkMd5":"d494db087de724d1a2b7e9e6b615ff89","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn45@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-25-077_eb944f5cfb257de2.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":173094,"destBytes":173094,"author":"Teresa Carey","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyNjQ1OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4ODUxNzM4OH0.9YlNp1cWI5iyBbOFqPmxfPBnwLCVY7aHvYmZp3UHJQs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C4%2C0%2C131&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn45@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-25-077_eb944f5cfb257de2.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyNjQ2MS9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NDQ1NDIxM30.STzdlLm-VL7AuA95JcnxRc3YfHFQQndyqyQkDjohu9g/img.png?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn80@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-445_571ed625d1ca34df.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/ZUXn6bmwhw0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn63@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-061_23887afa14d7a4be.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"As we listen to melodies, our brain guesses what's next","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/music-brain-predict","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTM3OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTg4OTkxMH0.5x0C-SfP6THG2KQIdEWcdc3IdVpuWfe0AbgAA0maQiY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>EEG recordings reveal that when we imagine melodies, our brains exhibit pretty much the same activity as when we listen to them.</li><li><span></span>When we listen to a melody and encounter a pause, our brains predict what note comes next — and then subconsciously check that against the next note that plays.</li><li>EEG activity is so closely aligned with melodies that researchers can identify a Bach melody being imagined.</li></ul><hr/><p>It seems that when it comes to the brain, music has its own set of keys to the place. (No pun intended.) Our brains, and particularly our emotions, respond to music in a uniquely direct manner that's not yet understood. A pair of new studies from researchers at the <a href=\"https://www.sfn.org/\" target=\"_blank\">Society for Neuroscience</a> report what happens electrically in the brain when we imagine or listen to music.Together, the two studies describe how listening to musical melodies activates an intriguing prediction/recognition system. When there is a pause between notes, the brain makes a prediction about the next note to come. Co-author Giovanni Di Liberto, speaking to <em>Inverse</em>, says the study is about more than just music — it provides a new look at how imagination may work.</p><h2>Why EEG?</h2><p>To eavesdrop on the brain's response to music, both studies utilized electroencephalogram (EEG) recordings of participants as they listened to Bach melodies. Although fMRI scans are often used in neuroscience, the authors of the study say they were interested in electrical activity — the EEG's turf — and not blood-oxygen levels, which is what fMRIs capture. EEGs capture data much more quickly than fMRIs, and in music, timing is everything.<br/></p><h2>Study 1: Imagining music</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"9abc33a7f3b866557e7895daac8e78af\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"2e2f1\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTM5Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MzQ1NzYyNH0.-tkbjk_s-CPrykMLusIWlewukgqLecmt6jIZ0itt4uY/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/@glitchlab\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Glitch Lab App</a>/Unsplash</small></p><p>\"Musical imagery is the voluntary internal hearing of music in the mind without the need for physical action or external stimulation,\" write the study authors.<br/></p><p>While previous research has shown that imagining music electrically activates certain areas of the brain, it has been unclear to what extent this activity resembles the electrical behavior of the brain when listening to music. Specifically, it was unknown whether music imagery involves an expectation of what note <em>should</em> come next, as happens when one is listening to music. Another question is whether music imagery encompasses the music's acoustics (that is, the way it sounds).</p><p>To answer these questions, in the <a href=\"https://www.jneurosci.org/content/early/2021/07/23/JNEUROSCI.0183-21.2021\" target=\"_blank\">first study</a>, the researchers used EEG to observe the brain activity of 21 professional musicians as they imagined Bach melodies.</p><p>Using regression analysis, the researchers found that \"imagined neural signals can be predicted accurately, similarly to the listening task, and were sufficiently robust to allow for accurate identification of the imagined musical piece from the EEG.\" Simply put, the scientists were able to identify the Bach songs the participants were imagining.</p><p>As it turns out, both imagining music and listening trigger similar frontal spatial activation. However, though the charges are similar, the EEG readings of the electrical polarity of the brain's neural responses flip — their polarity is positive for listening and negative for imagining. The researchers say this fits with listening's top-down prediction/recognition system observed in the second study.</p><p>Di Liberto explained to <em>Big Think</em> in an email, \"The positive vs. negative charge is what we measure with our technology and what allows us to disentangle sensory and prediction neural signals. The brain may not necessarily encode those as positive or negative,\" he notes, but the EEG signals may just be \"a byproduct of the brain subtracting (or inhibiting) the sensory response by the sensory prediction.\"</p><p>The researchers also observed \"overlapping but distinctive\" electrical responses to the acoustic properties of imagined versus heard music.</p><h2>Study 2: Listening's prediction and recognition routine</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"307994b1aa345414d29a93be6dce2e7c\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"50f2f\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTQwNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTM2OTcxMX0.hwJ_3n86_Zv6xpQJYo3QY8IiJEVMpj-4Pawe7BzlE0E/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/@armand_khoury\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Armand Khoury</a>/Unsplash</small></p><p>For the <a href=\"https://www.jneurosci.org/content/early/2021/07/22/JNEUROSCI.0184-21.2021\" target=\"_blank\">second study</a>, the researchers conducted two experiments in which participants listened to or imagined Bach melodies. Twenty participants participated in the first experiment and 21 people in the second. Once more, after EEG signals were lined up with the music's melodic structure, the same patterns were observed during listening and imagining Bach's notes, albeit again with their electrical polarity flipped.<br/></p><p>During pauses in the music, EEG activity continued, leading the researchers to conclude that the participants' brains were busy imagining the next note to be heard. The same type of activity occurred in silent moments of the songs when there could have been a note but there wasn't. There is no sensory input during silence and imagined music, so this activity comes from the brain's predictions.</p><p>When the predicted note (negative polarity) and the heard note (positive polarity) line up sufficiently, prediction and recognition align, and they cancel each other out electrically. When they do not, the heard note wins the electrical battle. \"We find that imagined predictive responses are routinely co-opted during ongoing music listening,\" when predictions prove inaccurate, write the authors.</p><h2>Why bother?</h2><p>Di Liberto said, \"Your brain responds more strongly to unexpected or unpredictable events.\" He told Big Think that this is literally true on an electrical level: \"The EEG signal has larger magnitude/power when participants hear a less predictable sound.\"</p><p>One might speculate that such little surprises are what make some pieces of music more memorable to us. This would make sense, since astute musicians understand that it is often the little surprises that make a song grab a listener. \"Wrong\" notes, momentary dissonances, and even unexpected noises are all part of a savvy musician's toolbox.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/TdkTzNuopIM\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 10 Aug 2021 16:26:10 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTM3OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTg4OTkxMH0.5x0C-SfP6THG2KQIdEWcdc3IdVpuWfe0AbgAA0maQiY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"a9eb39c0d7652bfa3a862c8278b5ab06","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn20@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-23-041_1680e53fefe99aba.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":294592,"destBytes":294592,"author":"Robby Berman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTM3OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTg4OTkxMH0.5x0C-SfP6THG2KQIdEWcdc3IdVpuWfe0AbgAA0maQiY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn20@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-23-041_1680e53fefe99aba.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTM5Ni9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MzQ1NzYyNH0.-tkbjk_s-CPrykMLusIWlewukgqLecmt6jIZ0itt4uY/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn75@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-27-965_2e2f187c2026904f.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2OTQwNC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTM2OTcxMX0.hwJ_3n86_Zv6xpQJYo3QY8IiJEVMpj-4Pawe7BzlE0E/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn17@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-28-102_50f2f68b7cd032a7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/TdkTzNuopIM":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn42@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-27-992_51ebb564042e8a20.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The power of utopian thinking: it's not just for naïve dreamers","link":"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/thomas-more-power-utopian-thinking","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1ODI3MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM5MjIyOH0.RC2IbFyiCoAStzOuzM7144bM73xpLKaTw3GCHJ6Ch64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C150&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Thomas More's <em>Utopia</em> presents an ideal society without money, property, violence, or inequality. It is science fiction, philosophy, and a political thought experiment rolled into one.</li><li>Many commentators today believe More's work is also a satire. His vision has dark and macabre undertones, and the idealism is so exaggerated as to be laughable.</li><li>Utopian thinking comes from many political directions, and this is a good thing — it is the impetus that pushes society forward.</li></ul><hr/><p>What would the perfect society look like to you? Try, if you can, to put aside how things are or what you think \"has to be\" the case, and just imagine your best possible world. What would the people be like? What jobs would people do? Who would get paid the most or live in the best houses? Would there even be money? Would there be any crime? Why not?</p><p>Sometimes a bit of utopian thinking is vitally important, and the very word (if not the idea) goes back to the 16th century intellectual, Thomas More.</p><h2>The land of milk and honey</h2><p>More's <em>Utopia</em> introduces a narrator who meets a traveller from the island of \"Utopia.\" This Utopian tells us about a perfect society, free from any inequality and violence. It is a place with no private property or unequal distribution of goods between the various segments of society. It is a place where jobs are divided fairly among the populace. It is a confederated city-state where violence is abhorred and is the worst of taboos and where \"all men zealously pursue the good of the public.\"</p><p>In Utopia, precious metals have lost their luster. This is done by deliberate negative association — the condemned are made to wear gold coronets or earrings as a shaming ritual. Toilets and tables are made of gold, making them so commonplace that no one would desire them to the point of harming another to attain them. Iron is much the more valued metal because it has a practical and useful role to play. Why would anyone want gaudy stones or overly malleable metals? Wealth is, instead, measured in \"cheerfulness, peace of mind, and freedom from anxiety.\"</p><p>Yet, there is a sinister aspect to this egalitarianism. If someone tries to leave the country, they are first imprisoned. If they try it again, they are turned into slaves. All the streets are identical, and after ten years, everyone has to swap houses by lot. All doors are open, and anyone can come and go as they please. Nothing is yours and nothing is mine.</p><h2>There's No-Place like Utopia</h2><p>Ask two scholars <em>why</em> More wrote Utopia, and you will get two different answers.</p><p>By one interpretation, it is subversive and revolutionary. In a post-Marx world, it is impossible to read <em>Utopia</em> without seeing the parallels to communism. Marx himself did occasionally make reference to More in a historical context but not to the ideas in Utopia. Engels and Marx regarded their work as a kind of socioeconomic science and would hate to have been labelled as \"utopian.\" They viewed such \"utopian socialists\" as selling only \"castles in the air\" that could never help the proletariat. </p><p>While More's <em>Utopia</em> would not be revolutionary in a Marxist sense, it would have been hugely contentious in its day. At the time More was writing — in a world of absolute monarchs, feudal peasants, and religious extremism — the ideas of egalitarianism and religious freedom were dangerously controversial.</p><p>In another interpretation, some see <em>Utopia</em> as being mocking and satirical. It could be that Utopia is <em>so</em> extreme at times as to become an absurdity or a parody. The Utopian world is portrayed as overbearing and invasive, and, in some respects, at odds with human nature. It could be that More was really saying, \"Look at these crazy ideas — they're nonsense!\" </p><p>This interpretation looks more likely when we realize that Utopia literally means \"no-place,\" the traveller's name (Hythloday) means \"nonsense,\" and the capital (Amaurot) means \"City of No Inhabitants.\"</p><h2>Utopian thinking: not just for dreamers</h2><p>But even this interpretation does not quite fit. Utopia is a provocation. It is an open question to us, the reader, about how society <em>should</em> be ordered. It inverts everything we assume and asks how things could be different. (And <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/library-of-babel\" target=\"_self\">everything really could be different</a>.) It is a thought experiment that throws much at the wall, and we are left to see what sticks.</p><p>Today, we live with the idea of utopianism, and we all have our own versions of Utopia. Is it one of science fiction, with humans living in distant galaxies? Is it one of nanotech and advanced AI? Is it one of a return to nature and respect for the environment? Is it communistic or anarchic? Is it the nostalgic golden age of the conservative or the quixotic idealism of a college sophomore? There are as many Utopias as there are people, but <em>what </em>makes someone's Utopia is not the point.</p><p>Utopianism is the bold and brave voice that challenges the status quo. The act of questioning brings about change. Utopianism is what dares to imagine a world that could be better. It is the forward path, lined with cynics and pessimists who ridicule the dreamers.</p><p>Perhaps the best way to view Utopia, and utopian thinking generally, is like a rainbow. The closer we move to it, the further it moves away. Although we will never reach it, its existence impels us to move forward.</p><p><em>Jonny Thomson teaches philosophy in Oxford. He runs a popular Instagram account called Mini Philosophy (@</em><a href=\"https://www.instagram.com/philosophyminis/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>philosophyminis</em></a><em>). His first book is </em><a href=\"https://www.amazon.co.uk/Mini-Philosophy-Small-Book-Ideas-ebook/dp/B08M3XDNPM/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Mini Philosophy: A Small Book of Big Ideas</em></a><em>.</em></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/42uG_7wfxEU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 09 Aug 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1ODI3MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM5MjIyOH0.RC2IbFyiCoAStzOuzM7144bM73xpLKaTw3GCHJ6Ch64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C150&height=700","linkMd5":"2cf42913a663ccb17fdf7845eabcde13","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn25@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-22-932_267aa656ad92e945.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":109524,"destBytes":109524,"author":"Jonny Thomson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1ODI3MC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM5MjIyOH0.RC2IbFyiCoAStzOuzM7144bM73xpLKaTw3GCHJ6Ch64/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C63%2C0%2C150&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn25@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-22-932_267aa656ad92e945.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/42uG_7wfxEU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn92@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-267_fbcc31e51022ab9a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588784},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Peeking behind a black hole: how matter bends space that bends light","link":"https://bigthink.com/13-8/black-hole-light-gravitational-lensing","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MDYyOTg5M30.DKO0ufXgXcVJUiOg7xzFnQM_Wb2Q5P7a9DkAYA18i1w/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>Einstein's theory of relativity predicts that matter bends space and that space bends light.</li><li>Since its confirmation in 1919, such light-bending has been used extensively to see the unseeable, like dark matter, which can bend space due to its gravity but does not shine.</li><li>Recently, scientists observed an even more dramatic effect: a black hole that bends space so intensely that it is possible to see light (X-rays) from behind it.</li></ul><hr/><p><span></span>In 1915, the 36-year-old Albert Einstein submitted his revolutionary description of gravity to the Berlin Academy of Sciences, a masterpiece of the human intellect that became known as the general theory of relativity. The journey had been long and hard, ten years in total, with many false starts and wrong turns. From then on, gravity, the universe, the nature of space and time, and their relation to matter and energy would never be the same.</p><h2>Newtonian gravity</h2><p>Until then, the prevalent theory of gravity was devised by Isaac Newton in 1687, just 192 years before Einstein's birth. The theory, incredibly powerful, described gravity in a somewhat mysterious way, positing that any two massive bodies attract each other with a force proportional to their masses and inversely proportional to the square of their distance. This action-at-a-distance bothered Newton, who did not venture an explanation of the fact that his force was an instantaneous interaction across space. How could the sun attract Earth and you attract your computer without touching? How could this interaction be instantaneous? Still, the theory worked, and that is what mattered.</p><h2>Special and general relativity</h2><p>Until it didn't. By the late 19<sup>th</sup> century, anomalies in the orbit of Mercury could not be explained applying Newton's force. Einstein was convinced that nothing could be instantaneous in the universe. The limited speed was that of light, and correcting for this changed everything. In 1905, he showed that for two observers moving at constant speeds with respect to each other, they would measure distances and times differently. A moving stick was shorter, a moving clock ticked slower. This was his special theory of relativity, special for being confined to motion at constant speeds. The next step, he quickly realized, was to generalize this theory to objects that could accelerate, like pretty much everything. And here came the surprise.</p><p>In what Einstein deemed \"the happiest thought of my life,\" he realized that a freely falling body would not feel its own weight. (That is why you feel \"lighter\" when going down a fast elevator. The downward acceleration changes the net pull from Earth's gravity. If the elevator falls, you just float, albeit with a tragic outcome.) From this vision, Einstein concluded that gravity and acceleration are indistinguishable. The pushing and pulling of accelerated motion could, in principle, be mimicked by placing masses in front of or behind you.</p><p>Now, imagine a cannonball shot from the top of a hill. It will trace a parabolic curve until it hits its target down below. That is gravity accelerating the cannonball downward. Following his vision, Einstein turned things upside-down.</p><p>What if, instead of thinking of the cannonball describing a curved path in an inert flat space, we imagined space itself being curved? The larger the mass concentration, the more it would bend space, corresponding to a larger acceleration due to gravity. Mercury, being the planet closest to the sun, would have its orbit distorted by the curved space around it. When Einstein applied his theory to Mercury, he discovered that the <a href=\"https://aether.lbl.gov/www/classes/p10/gr/PrecessionperihelionMercury.htm\" target=\"_blank\">planet's orbital anomaly</a> could be described beautifully.</p><h2>Einstein becomes a star</h2>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d4f36a9e5c5909eb54ef1bc996ab6fb5\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"69b70\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY3OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTU2MDQzNn0.nowOMd2C8OKGwxDdwxBAIA8syyM51HoluV1jd6jdox0/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: sakkmesterke / Adobe Stock</small></p>\n        <p>So, Einstein's theory served as a correction to Newton's for objects with large enough masses. Although every massive body distorts space around its vicinity (including you), the effect is tiny. But stars, large agglomerates of mass, and most dramatically, black holes, can bend space in very noticeable ways.<br/></p><p>The most immediate consequence of a bent space is that light, always searching for the shortest path between two points, will follow the spatial curvature: a ray of light will curve along a bent space. So, Einstein predicted that mass would bend space that would bend light itself. Amazing.</p><p>This bending of light was confirmed in 1919 during a solar eclipse, catapulting Einstein to international stardom overnight. Just as a lens, a piece of curved glass, bends light so that people can see better, space will also act as a lens, distorting the paths of light from distant stars and other sources of light visible and invisible to the human eye.</p><p>This effect, known as gravitational lensing, is well-known and already documented in many spectacular images, from the bending of light from distant stars as they pass by the sun to Einstein's prediction of spectacular bright arcs surrounding galaxies. Light beams from a distant source are bent mid-course by a massive object or objects. <a href=\"https://science.sciencemag.org/content/84/2188/506\" target=\"_blank\">Einstein predicted</a> such <a href=\"https://www.nrao.edu/pr/2000/vla20/background/ering/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">arcs or rings</a> in 1936. This is how, for example, it is possible to estimate how much dark matter there is in a galaxy cluster. Since dark matter only interacts with normal matter (stuff made of atoms like we are) through gravity, it will bend space around it. The amount of bending can then be used to estimate how much invisible dark matter is hiding in the galaxy or galaxy cluster.</p><h2>Einstein continues to amaze us</h2><p>A few weeks back, a <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/black-hole-x-ray\" target=\"_self\">new spectacular effect due to the bending of light</a> was observed by a group led by Dan Wilkins from Stanford University. Not only visible light can be bent by gravity; any kind of electromagnetic radiation will follow the curved path of space around a mass. Of course, nothing could be more dramatic than what happens around a giant black hole; light gets severely bent to the point that we can even see what is behind the black hole itself. Imagine that, seeing what is <em>behind</em> a body.</p><p>In the recent discovery, the swirling matter being sucked into the black hole superheats, separating electrons from atoms. This spinning disk of hot plasma generates powerful magnetic fields that arc around the black hole and split in places. The light seen is in the form of X-rays, appearing as fainter echoes of the more dramatic action in front of the hole.</p><p>Once again, Einstein's happiest thought generated something spectacular and fully in agreement with the predictions from his theory. Although he was not a fan of black holes — or of any object or phenomenon that hinted at the breakdown of the laws of nature, as happens inside a black hole — he certainly would celebrate one more goal scored by his remarkable theory. After 106 years, general relativity continues to amaze us every day.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Okm865tVqNM\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MDYyOTg5M30.DKO0ufXgXcVJUiOg7xzFnQM_Wb2Q5P7a9DkAYA18i1w/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"ed813ef6e6dbf4ccc717a5269be90bbe","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn52@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-236_b99af85775746e6a.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":74474,"destBytes":74474,"author":"Marcelo Gleiser","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MDYyOTg5M30.DKO0ufXgXcVJUiOg7xzFnQM_Wb2Q5P7a9DkAYA18i1w/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn52@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-21-236_b99af85775746e6a.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MjY3OC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTYyOTU2MDQzNn0.nowOMd2C8OKGwxDdwxBAIA8syyM51HoluV1jd6jdox0/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn1@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-28-111_69b70c14a328ba12.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Okm865tVqNM":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn53@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-27-989_fe9b42d1275ea5f6.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"How to trick your brain into saving money","link":"https://bigthink.com/singleton/saving-money","description":"<iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"100%\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.rebelmouse.com/res/scraper/embed/?jwplayer_video_url=https%3A%2F%2Fcontent.jwplatform.com%2Fplayers%2F6PeUBsPE-FvQKszTI.js\" width=\"100%\"></iframe><br/><ul><li>Thanks to evolution, humans are not great at thinking and planning long term. \"The human brain just has certain tendencies,\" says neuroscientist Alex Korb. \"We're always going to pay more attention to things that are immediate right now.\" Luckily, there are ways to counteract our natural habits.</li><li>Retirement can feel abstract—especially for young people who are just starting in their careers—which makes preparing for it less of a priority. By understanding how the brain impacts certain decisions, we can hack those biases to develop better saving practices and work toward more concrete, tangible goals.</li><li>\"Saving one dollar a day is infinitely better than saving zero dollars a day,\" says Korb. Building on small decisions over time gives you the ability to course correct if strategies are not working out, and leaves room for meaningful progress at your own pace.</li></ul><div><br/></div><hr/><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/TM8WmirhgcU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 10 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"b57fb36b1bed7e7947ddf00dc72c47f8","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Big Think","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/TM8WmirhgcU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn59@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-27-984_98ed720642edd364.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588781},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The metaverse: From science fiction to virtual reality","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/metaverse","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQ4Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2OTA3MDExMX0.PIdBDr9IIfceGI92W5K8s3QZt-ipg9J0K6jpkaBvP-8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C66%2C0%2C484&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p>The metaverse is a network of always-on virtual environments in which many people can interact with one another and digital objects while operating virtual representations – or avatars – of themselves.</p><hr/><p>\n\t Think of a combination of <a href=\"https://www.wired.com/story/wired-guide-to-virtual-reality/\" target=\"_blank\">immersive virtual reality</a>, a <a href=\"https://www.lifewire.com/the-top-mmorpgs-813063\" target=\"_blank\">massively multiplayer online role-playing game</a> and the web.\n</p><p>\n\tThe metaverse is a concept from science fiction that many people in the technology industry envision as the successor to today's internet. It's only a vision at this point, but technology companies like Facebook are aiming to make it the setting for many online activities, including work, play, studying and shopping.\n</p><p>\n\tMetaverse is a portmanteau of meta, meaning transcendent, and verse, from universe. Sci-fi novelist Neal Stephenson coined the term in his 1992 novel “<a href=\"https://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/172832/snow-crash-by-neal-stephenson/\" target=\"_blank\">Snow Crash</a>\" to describe the virtual world in which the protagonist, Hiro Protagonist, socializes, shops and vanquishes real-world enemies through his avatar. The concept predates “Snow Crash\" and was popularized as “cyberspace\" in William Gibson's groundbreaking 1984 novel “<a href=\"https://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/538861/neuromancer-by-william-gibson/\" target=\"_blank\">Neuromancer</a>.\"\n</p><p>\n\tThere are three key aspects of the metaverse: presence, interoperability and standardization.\n</p><p>\n\tPresence is the feeling of actually being in a virtual space, with virtual others. <a href=\"https://ispr.info/\" target=\"_blank\">Decades of research</a> has shown that this sense of embodiment improves the quality of online interactions. This sense of presence is achieved through virtual reality technologies such as head-mounted displays.\n</p><p>\n\tInteroperability means being able to seamlessly travel between virtual spaces with the same virtual assets, such as avatars and digital items. <a href=\"https://readyplayer.me/\" target=\"_blank\">ReadyPlayerMe</a> allows people to create an avatar that they can use in hundreds of different virtual worlds, including in Zoom meetings through apps like <a href=\"https://www.animaze.us/\" target=\"_blank\">Animaze</a>. Meanwhile, <a href=\"http://graphics.reuters.com/TECHNOLOGY-BLOCKCHAIN/010070P11GN/index.html\" target=\"_blank\">blockchain</a> technologies such as <a href=\"https://www.investopedia.com/terms/c/cryptocurrency.asp\" target=\"_blank\">cryptocurrencies</a> and <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/how-nonfungible-tokens-work-and-where-they-get-their-value-a-cryptocurrency-expert-explains-nfts-157489\" target=\"_blank\">nonfungible tokens</a> facilitate the transfer of digital goods across virtual borders.\n</p><p>\n\tStandardization is what enables interoperability of platforms and services across the metaverse. As with all mass-media technologies – from the printing press to texting – common technological standards are essential for widespread adoption. International organizations such as the <a href=\"https://www.w3.org/community/metaverse-interop/\" target=\"_blank\">Open Metaverse Interoperability Group</a> define these standards.\n</p><h2>Why the metaverse matters</h2><p>\n\tIf the metaverse does become the successor to the internet, who builds it, and how, is extremely important to the future of the economy and society as a whole. Facebook is aiming to play a leading role in shaping the metaverse, in part by <a href=\"https://doi.org/10.1177%2F1461444820960411\" target=\"_blank\">investing heavily in virtual reality</a>. Facebook CEO Mark Zuckerberg <a href=\"https://www.theverge.com/22588022/mark-zuckerberg-facebook-ceo-metaverse-interview\" target=\"_blank\">explained in an interview</a> his view that the metaverse spans non-immersive platforms like today's social media as well as immersive 3D media technologies such as virtual reality, and that it <a href=\"https://www.theverge.com/2020/5/21/21266945/facebook-ar-vr-remote-work-oculus-passthrough-future-tech\" target=\"_blank\">will be for work</a> as well as play.\n</p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"260\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/cSp1dM2Vj48?wmode=transparent&start=0\" width=\"440\">\n</iframe><p>\n\t            Hollywood has embraced the metaverse in movies like 'Ready Player One.'\n</p><p>\n\tThe metaverse might one day resemble the flashy fictional Oasis of Ernest Cline's “<a href=\"https://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/209887/ready-player-one-by-ernest-cline/\" target=\"_blank\">Ready Player One</a>,\" but until then you can turn to games like <a href=\"https://www.epicgames.com/fortnite/en-US/home\" target=\"_blank\">Fortnite</a> and <a href=\"https://www.roblox.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Roblox</a>, virtual reality social media platforms like <a href=\"https://hello.vrchat.com/\" target=\"_blank\">VRChat</a> and <a href=\"https://altvr.com/\" target=\"_blank\">AltspaceVR</a>, and virtual work environments like <a href=\"https://immersed.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Immersed</a> for a taste of the immersive and connected metaverse experience. As these siloed spaces converge and become increasingly interoperable, watch for a truly singular metaverse to emerge.\n</p><p>\n\t<a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/rabindra-ratan-269916\" target=\"_blank\">Rabindra Ratan</a>, Associate Professor of Media and Information, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/michigan-state-university-1349\" target=\"_blank\">Michigan State University</a></em> and <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/yiming-lei-1257934\" target=\"_blank\">Yiming Lei</a>, Doctoral student in Media and Information, <em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/michigan-state-university-1349\" target=\"_blank\">Michigan State University</a></em>\n</p><p>\n\tThis article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\" target=\"_blank\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQ4Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2OTA3MDExMX0.PIdBDr9IIfceGI92W5K8s3QZt-ipg9J0K6jpkaBvP-8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C66%2C0%2C484&height=700","linkMd5":"59e61d2c110ec342eca668f696a3af9b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn3@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-23-229_9fe75e412cf8681b.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":39786,"destBytes":39786,"author":"Rabindra Ratan & Yiming Lei","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIyMjQ4Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2OTA3MDExMX0.PIdBDr9IIfceGI92W5K8s3QZt-ipg9J0K6jpkaBvP-8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C66%2C0%2C484&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn3@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-23-229_9fe75e412cf8681b.webp","https://counter.theconversation.com/content/165731/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-advanced":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn83@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-40-113_1335aa4c694dd30b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/zLQb39s1sGE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn44@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-27-997_68015acce53d0a56.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"This chart shows when each new sport joined the Olympic Games","link":"https://bigthink.com/culture-religion/olympic-sports-timeline","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2MDkxNS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NDk0NDgxN30.gQbmKK22qv7OcGkTdvZou4LEt_h-eygS5NxEv9x8cqU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C308%2C0%2C309&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p>This year's <a href=\"https://www.statista.com/topics/6266/history-of-the-olympics/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Olympic Summer Games</a> in Tokyo will see the addition of five new sports, out of which three will stick around.</p><hr/><p> Skateboarding, climbing and surfing will be added to the Olympics' roster, <a href=\"https://www.topendsports.com/events/summer/sports/changes.htm\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">while karate and baseball/softball will go the route of many other sports which have made short-lived appearances over the years.</a></p><p>Our chart shows <a href=\"https://olympics.com/en/sports/summer-olympics\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">new additions</a> since 1984 that have stuck around – some reflecting new trends in sporting, while others were added by host countries trying to highlight their strong points.</p><p>In the flair of the 1980s, artistic swimming and rhythmic gymnastics introduced colorful outfits and contemporary music to the event. The 1990s newly introduced trends like beach volleyball and mountain biking, while the 2000s did the same with trampolining, triathlon and BMX. The only sports added in the 2010s era were rugby and golf, re-introduced to the Olympics at the 2016 games in Rio de Janeiro.</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"0b235a7b5e8675b3dcb22ce6a4d53b4c\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"f6acf\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2MDg5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1NjA0MzU2M30.IiZWK-L2jIfwtCmOH3C3inEil6cwwuJQbg3xEq_JNtc/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Image: Statista</small></p><p>Attentive observers will detect another trend in new Olympic additions. In 1988, table tennis debuted in Seoul. It being a popular sport in many Asian countries, South Korea has since bagged 18 medals in the competition, including combined gold and silver medals in the men's singles during the inaugural event. The same pattern is apparent for the additions of Judo in 1964 in Tokyo, handball in 1936 in Berlin or boxing in 1904 in St. Louis. All events led to at least one inaugural gold medal.<br/></p><p>More than 30 new additions have stuck since the Olympics of the modern age started in 1896 in Athens. That year, the Olympic staples athletics and gymnastics were on the schedule, while competitions in current Olympic sports fencing, cycling, shooting, swimming, tennis, weightlifting and wrestling also took place. Seven more current-era sports were added four years later in Paris, including rowing and equestrian sports.</p><p><span></span>Several other disciplines did not establish themselves. The most famous discontinued sport might be tug-of-war, which was featured from 1896 until 1920, but it's by no means the only curious one. Lacrosse was played in 1904 and 1908, while athletes competed in polo on and off between 1896 and 1936. The 1908 Olympics in London even included a competition in water motorsports. Other than rugby and golf, which were featured several times in the early 1900s before reappearing in 2016, tennis took the longest hiatus between 1928 and 1984 (13 games), followed by archery from 1924 to 1968 (10 games). The only sports that have featured in all Olympic Summer Games since 1896 are gymnastics, athletics, cycling, fencing and swimming. Rowing and water polo have been continuously featured since their inaugurations in 1900.</p><p>Reprinted with permission of the <a href=\"https://www.weforum.org/\" target=\"_blank\">World Economic Forum</a>. src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/M2kQe304wfk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 09 Aug 2021 06:57:28 Buchholz","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2MDkxNS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4NDk0NDgxN30.gQbmKK22qv7OcGkTdvZou4LEt_h-eygS5NxEv9x8cqU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C308%2C0%2C309&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn21@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-23-721_9df70b9e8eaf3175.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2MDg5OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1NjA0MzU2M30.IiZWK-L2jIfwtCmOH3C3inEil6cwwuJQbg3xEq_JNtc/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn35@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-28-000_f6acfd5bf95bc759.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/M2kQe304wfk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn93@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-27-985_a53ce276386d8369.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588788},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The “Great Dimming” of Betelgeuse explained","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/great-dimming-betelgeuse-explained","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDYzNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjQzMTk2OX0.M_XdxqhJcoPm6ffg8Yg8xBNqgBR2HeNbQ2JagAC7QV0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=71%2C0%2C71%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Last year, Betelgeuse dimmed by a third before returning to normal.</li><li>A number of studies blame some combination of cooling and dust.</li><li>The star is not set to explode anytime soon, much to the disappointment of many.</li></ul><hr/><p>One of the most notable stars in the night sky is Betelgeuse. Typically the tenth brightest star in the night sky, it is well known for its red color and prominent place in the constellation Orion. Its name, which like many other stars comes to us from Arabic, translates to \"the arm of the giant\" or \"the armpit of the great one,\" which is an excellent description of where it is located in that constellation. </p><p>As a semiregular variable star, it can undergo shifts in its brightness of varying intensity for irregular lengths of time. Scientists were not entirely surprised by an extended dimming of the star between November 2019 and March 2020. However, the magnitude of that dimming, a 35 percent drop, was remarkable — so much so that more than a few people began to speculate that the star was about to explode (or \"go supernova\" in astronomical parlance).</p><p>Astronomers all over the world turned their telescopes toward Betelgeuse in the hope of figuring out what was going on. A few recently published papers provide some explanations.</p><h2>A tale of two studies</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/aR5nfB1Dagg\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>One recent<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41467-021-25018-3\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>paper</u></a>, published in <em>Nature Communications </em>by a team composed mostly of Chinese scientists, argues that the dimming was caused by a large, dark, cooler spot on the star.</p><p>To determine if the star was cooler during its dimming, the team devised a method for measuring titanium oxide and cyanide molecules in the<a href=\"https://casswww.ucsd.edu/archive/public/tutorial/Stars.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>star's spectra</u></a> — an analysis of the intensity of various wavelengths of light given off by a star that can reveal things like temperature, chemical composition, and mass. Titanium oxide and cyanide are more common in cooler stars, as they tend to disintegrate in hotter ones.</p><p>Their data suggested that Betelgeuse cooled by 170 Kelvin, from 3646 K (6103 F°) to 3476 K (5797 F°) during the dimming period. They suggest that such a dramatic cooling could be caused by a dark spot on the star, a well known phenomenon often caused by large convective cells on the stellar surface.</p><p>Because it is unlikely that the entire star cooled that much, the researchers further suggest that the dark spot itself was fairly large and as much as 340 K cooler than the rest of the star. Some other researchers concur with this <a href=\"https://iopscience.iop.org/article/10.3847/2041-8213/ab9ca6\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>proposal</u></a>, but this is not the only potential explanation.</p><p>Another<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-021-03546-8\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>study</u></a>, published in <em>Nature</em> and conducted by an international team of researchers, suggests that a combination of dust and cooling was at play. They propose that this dust patch was caused, in part, by a cool patch similar to the one discussed in the aforementioned study.</p><p>Observations from shortly before the dimming period began suggest that some mass, perhaps in the form of a gas cloud, was lost from Betelgeuse. The formation of a cool spot could allow the dust in the cloud to condense and clump together, obscuring the star. The star would regain its brightness after the cool spot dissolved and the dust blew away. </p><p>The authors further conclude that the models for cooling and dust do not conflict and that a combination of the two best fits the observations. While differing on a few details, including the overall importance of <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2021/06/17/science/betelgeuse-montarges-star-supernova.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>dust</u></a>, the two studies largely agree on how much Betelgeuse cooled and how that cooling impacted our ability to see it. </p><h2>Betelgeuse! Betelgeuse! Betelgeuse!</h2><p>The star, while having long since returned to a more typical brightness, continues to fluctuate in brightness, though nothing on the scale of the previous \"Great Dimming\" event. </p><p>Despite speculation that the star was about to explode, it is not expected to do so for another 100,000 years. However, when it does, it is going to die in spectacular fashion — likely shining brighter than Venus for a short time before collapsing on itself and turning into a neutron star or black hole.</p><p>If you're going to pick a lucky star, maybe don't pick that one. </p><p><br/></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/ky4DWUmxEco\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Aug 2021 17:09:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDYzNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjQzMTk2OX0.M_XdxqhJcoPm6ffg8Yg8xBNqgBR2HeNbQ2JagAC7QV0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=71%2C0%2C71%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"e77f3be16a5604875bdb00cd114f3bef","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn38@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-400_4919ce6135fcef04.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":9526,"destBytes":9526,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3NDYzNy9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3NjQzMTk2OX0.M_XdxqhJcoPm6ffg8Yg8xBNqgBR2HeNbQ2JagAC7QV0/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=71%2C0%2C71%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn38@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-400_4919ce6135fcef04.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/ky4DWUmxEco":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn29@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-983_0caec9b4d525e4be.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Solved: A 50-year mystery about Jupiter","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/jupiter-heat-aurora","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2NjU4MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MDM2MjEwNX0.AfMaWF-URzEpKaq49Ygo3PYOeBq1tr6elH6kP_ymmTA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=299%2C0%2C299%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>The source of most of Jupiter's heat has baffled scientists for decades.</li><li>A new study suggests that Jovian auroras provide the energy that heats the upper atmosphere.</li><li>The findings confirm a mechanism long suspected but never proven to be at work. </li></ul><hr/><p>Much like the Earth, Jupiter has had a sort of \"energy crisis\" for the last few decades relating to global temperatures. Compared to how warm the planet should be given how far it is from the sun, Jupiter has a remarkable amount of heat. The cause of this disconnect has troubled scientists for decades, though several possible ideas for the cause have been proposed.</p>\n<p>This month, a solution has finally been found. In a study published in<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-021-03706-w\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u><em>Nature</em></u></a>, a team of researchers using data from the Keck II telescope and several satellites has determined that the <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/jupiter-aurora\" target=\"_self\"><u>incredibly powerful Jovian aurora</u></a> is heating the upper atmosphere to extremely high temperatures.</p>\n<h2>Some like it hot</h2><p>According to<a href=\"https://www.nasa.gov/feature/goddard/2021/juno-jupiter-auroral-heating\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>NASA</u></a>, we should expect Jupiter's upper atmosphere to be at a temperature of about -73 C° (-100 F°) given how little sunlight it receives. However, observation after observation points to a blazing average temperature closer to 426 C° (800 F°). </p><p>Existing heat maps of Jupiter's atmospheric temperature were sparse in detail, necessitating more precise information to solve the puzzle of where the heat was coming from. By combining observational data from the Keck II telescope and information on Jupiter's magnetic field from the Hisaki and Juno satellites, the team was able to observe bursts of energy moving from the polar aurora down toward the equatorial regions of Jupiter on two occasions.</p><p>By chance, the nights the instruments observed Jupiter coincided with a considerable solar wind that reached the planet, improving the data. Heat from the electrically charged particles could be tracked with ease as that heat moved outward from the poles in pulses. The data show the heat is widely distributed, centered around the poles, and gradually declining at lower latitudes. </p><h2>Confirming what was long suspected</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/JEU3ppMIziI\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>The strength of Jupiter's electromagnetic field — 20,000 times stronger than Earth's — has been known for some time. The frequency and intensity of its<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/jupiter-aurora\" target=\"_self\"> <u>aurora</u></a>, some of which is fueled by eruptions from the moon Io, has also been established. However, while this was proposed as a possible mechanism for the observed temperatures, traditional models of gas giants presume that Coriolis forces (the effect caused by planetary rotation that causes weather patterns to veer off rather than move in a straight line) would be able to keep auroral energy confined to the poles.</p><p>The study's lead author Dr. James O'Donoghue explained how long this process <a href=\"https://phys.org/news/2021-08-space-scientists-reveal-secret-jupiter.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>took</u></a>:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 40px;\">\"We first began trying to create a global heat map of Jupiter's uppermost atmosphere at the University of Leicester. The signal was not bright enough to reveal anything outside of Jupiter's polar regions at the time, but with the lessons learned from that work we managed to secure time on one of the largest, most competitive telescopes on Earth some years later.\"</p><p>Co-author Dr. Tom Stallard of the School of Physics and Astronomy at the University of Leicester placed the findings in further context:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 40px;\">\"This 'energy crisis' has been a long standing issue — do the models fail to properly model how heat flows from the aurora, or is there some other unknown heat source near the equator? This paper describes how we have mapped this region in unprecedented detail and have shown that, at Jupiter, the equatorial heating is directly associated with auroral heating.\"</p><p>Other theories on why the atmosphere is so hot include acoustic waves from the planet's interior or gravity waves providing the needed energy. While this study did not entirely rule these models out, it does suggest that the primary mechanism is the aurora.</p><p><br/></p><img
