Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2NjU4MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY5MDM2MjEwNX0.AfMaWF-URzEpKaq49Ygo3PYOeBq1tr6elH6kP_ymmTA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=299%2C0%2C299%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn31@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-23-065_6266982b59215acf.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/nXOmTVBrKio":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn27@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-304_6ec7a5e8c74bce59.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Light from other side of black hole shows Einstein is right (again)","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/black-hole-x-ray","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTA4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzUyNDk2NH0.eZGpdg3-nWwD_uBu68JnyHcLtylJWPYfDal4MDJwGMg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C371%2C0%2C573&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>A new study shows how X-rays bent behind a black hole can be observed by telescopes. </li><li>The findings were predicted by the theory of general relativity but never previously observed. </li><li>The researchers plan to develop a 3D model.</li></ul><hr/><p>Few interstellar objects entice the imagination quite like black holes, massive objects with gravity so powerful nothing — not even light — can escape them. Once you go in, barring the existence of a wormhole to somewhere else, you are not coming back out. (For a scientific but fictional narrative of what it would be like to fall into a black hole, read this <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/13-8/what-happens-falling-black-hole\" target=\"_self\"><u>essay</u></a> by Marcelo Gleiser.)</p>\n<p>But now, a new<a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-021-03667-0\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>study</u></a> investigating the wild effects these objects have on magnetic fields and superheated gases has revealed the seemingly impossible: light from behind a black hole.</p><h2>How to see a black hole</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/KzhtNQAkItU\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>As you might expect from their primary claim to fame, it is difficult to directly observe black holes. The light that would typically be reflected off an object, allowing us to see it, simply falls into the hole.</p><p>However, Einstein told us that gravity works less like a giant vacuum cleaner sucking things toward large bodies and more like a heavy object placed on a rubber sheet. Gravity is the dip in the sheet near the object. Things moving past this dip, be it caused by a star or black hole, can pass by it without necessarily falling into it, though their paths will be altered.</p><p>Similarly, light — which consists of both visible and invisible portions of the electromagnetic spectrum — emitted by distant objects can be warped or bent slightly by large objects. This makes the object that emitted the light appear to be in a different place to an observer. This phenomenon, known as \"<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_lens\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>gravitational lensing</u></a>,\" was used to validate elements of relativity a century ago: light from distant stars was bent when passing by the sun, just as Einstein predicted. </p><p>When astronomers examine black holes, they tend to take advantage of this warping. For instance, they look for distant light that is bent or focused by the black hole or to X-rays emitted from near the black hole (but far enough away not to fall in) to ascertain information about the black hole, such as its location.</p><p>This new study began in much the same way. A team of researchers was examining the coronas that form around black holes as material falls into them. This superheated material turns to plasma (ionized gas) as it falls in, though some of it is tossed about by the black hole's spin and magnetic field. This turmoil continuously produces huge amounts of vibrant X-ray light around the hole, which can be observed to learn more about it. </p><p><img src=\"https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/KSgh0HNJOnOVv41YfM_r0CPBRYQtilv_OpteOQ3XoAJMBRObqxX17coQe_bebMIgJuBEVRiMgMGDiImcyUzBKlPXQ5BFsC_1TCAD7XPhR6Y1ZSDWYzqwtLk_7LdvNiAunLOHoEXk\"/></p><p><em>ESA</em><br/></p><p>While observing the expected X-Rays, the researchers noticed something odd; smaller, strangely \"colored\" X-ray flashes. The flashes were in line with predictions that X-rays would be bent and reflected behind the black hole before becoming visible again as \"echoes\" of light. </p><p>As Stanford University astrophysicist<a href=\"https://profiles.stanford.edu/wilkinsd\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>Dan Wilkins</u></a> and study co-author explains, this was both a surprise and in line with <a href=\"https://news.stanford.edu/2021/07/28/first-detection-light-behind-black-hole/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>theory</u></a>:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 40px;\">\"Any light that goes into that black hole doesn't come out, so we shouldn't be able to see anything that's behind the black hole. The reason we can see that is because that black hole is warping space, bending light and twisting magnetic fields around itself.\"</p><p>While relativity tells us that we should be able to see X-rays from behind a black hole because of its powerful gravity, this has never actually been seen before now. Study co-author<a href=\"https://profiles.stanford.edu/roger-blandford\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>Roger Blandford</u></a> of Stanford explained in a<a href=\"https://news.stanford.edu/2021/07/28/first-detection-light-behind-black-hole/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> <u>press release</u></a> why finding it now is so exciting for those who have long worked on the problem:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 40px;\">\"Fifty years ago, when astrophysicists starting [sic] speculating about how the magnetic field might behave close to a black hole, they had no idea that one day we might have the techniques to observe this directly and see Einstein's general theory of relativity in action.\"</p><p>The initial investigations into the coronas of black holes will continue, likely using more powerful X-ray telescopes as they become available. There are also <a href=\"https://www.nbcnews.com/science/space/researchers-see-light-echo-black-hole-first-time-proving-einstein-righ-rcna1553\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>plans</u></a> for making a 3D model to map these findings. The theory of relativity likely will continue to prove its worth for the foreseeable future. </p><p><br/></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/8EKEeoDnUFY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Mon, 09 Aug 2021 12:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTA4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzUyNDk2NH0.eZGpdg3-nWwD_uBu68JnyHcLtylJWPYfDal4MDJwGMg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C371%2C0%2C573&height=700","linkMd5":"b4b567d19b4a9d17ec5776ddb1a99ba1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn41@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-21-130_4526f7781f1f4309.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":8926,"destBytes":8926,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE1OTA4My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NzUyNDk2NH0.eZGpdg3-nWwD_uBu68JnyHcLtylJWPYfDal4MDJwGMg/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C371%2C0%2C573&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn41@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-21-130_4526f7781f1f4309.webp","https://lh5.googleusercontent.com/KSgh0HNJOnOVv41YfM_r0CPBRYQtilv_OpteOQ3XoAJMBRObqxX17coQe_bebMIgJuBEVRiMgMGDiImcyUzBKlPXQ5BFsC_1TCAD7XPhR6Y1ZSDWYzqwtLk_7LdvNiAunLOHoEXk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn51@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-088_9fc5d3d8a2d94a7d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/8EKEeoDnUFY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn65@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-28-171_4cd05ec25219df92.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588785},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The other moral in Frankenstein and how to apply it to human brains and reanimated pigs","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/moral-of-frankenstein","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDQ3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjcxNzA5Nn0.Db4NBbbUNQznPOqbJm_JJIQYUkOT2w4lq4Cegpau-m8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C180%2C0%2C532&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>Two bioethicists consider a lesser known moral in <em>Frankenstein</em> and what it means for science today.</li><li>We are still a ways from Shelley's novel, but we are getting closer.</li><li>They suggest that scientists begin thinking of sentient creations as having moral rights regardless of what the law says. </li></ul><hr/><p>Compared to what we see in science fiction, most of our major advances in modifying organisms are actually rather dull. For example, despite being deemed \"Frankenfoods,\" genetically modified crops are typically changed in simple ways that make them hardier and easier to grow. We are still a long way from approaching the work of Dr. Frankenstein.</p><p>However, we are getting closer all the time. And exactly what we should do in the event that we create an organism with moral standing remains the subject of some debate.</p><p>Because of this, Dr. Julian Koplin of the University of Melbourne Law School and Dr. John Massie of The Royal Children's Hospital in Melbourne wrote a <a href=\"https://jme.bmj.com/content/47/8/567\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>paper</u></a> discussing a lesser known ethical lesson of <em>Frankenstein</em> and how it might be applied to some of our more cutting-edge experiments — <em>before</em> we find ourselves asking what to do with artificially created sentient life.</p><h2>The other moral in Frankenstein</h2><p>The moral of Mary Shelley's <em>Frankenstein</em> that most people are familiar with is, \"Don't play God,\" or some variation of that theme. Most film and television versions of the story follow this route, perhaps most notably in the famous <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=BqF28KiD-Hc\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>1931 film</u></a> adaptation starring Boris Karloff as the monster.</p><blockquote>This take on the ethical lesson of Frankenstein may be more useful than the broad warning against hubris, as modern science is getting ever closer to creating things with sentience.</blockquote><p>However, Shelly's work covers many themes. One of them is that the real moral failure of Victor Frankenstein was not in creating his creature but in failing to meet or even consider the moral obligations he had to it. Thus, your pedantic friend who notes, \"Frankenstein is the name of the doctor, not the monster,\" is both annoying and correct. Frankenstein never bothered to name his creature after bringing it into the world.</p><p>That's not the only thing Frankenstein failed to give the creature. The authors explain:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 40px;\">\"...the 'monster' had at least some degree of moral status — which is to say, he was the kind of being to which we have moral obligations. Frankenstein refused to recognize <em>any</em> duties towards his creation, including even the modest duties we currently extend towards nonhuman research animals; Frankenstein denied his creature a name, shelter, healthcare, citizenship, or relationships with other creatures of its kind. In so doing, Frankenstein wronged his creation.\"</p><p>The Creature, as the monster is sometimes known in the novel, differs greatly from how most films depict him — uncoordinated, stupid, and brutish. He learns to speak several languages, references classic literature, and reveals that he is a vegetarian for ethical reasons. Before he spends his time devising a complex revenge plot against his creator, his primary desire is for companionship. He is also quite sensitive. Even if he is not entitled to the same moral standing as other humans, it seems intuitive that he has some moral standing that is never recognized. </p><p>This take on the ethical lesson of Frankenstein may be more useful than the broad warning against hubris, as modern science is getting ever closer to creating things with sentience. </p><h2>Brain experiments are getting creepy and weird</h2><p>One area of experimentation is the creation of <a href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fcell.2020.579659/full\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>human brain organoids</u></a> that provide simplified, living 3D models of the brain. These organoids are grown with stem cells over the course of several months and are very similar to certain parts of the cortex. Scientists are doing this in their effort to better understand the brain and its associated diseases. </p><p>While it is unlikely that we have created anything complex enough to achieve consciousness, many researchers maintain that it is theoretically possible for an organoid to become conscious. Some experiments have already produced tissues that are light <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5659341/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>sensitive</u></a>, suggesting at least a limited capacity for awareness.</p><p>In a turn toward a more literal reading of Shelley, a team of Yale scientists reanimated <a href=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/zombie-pigs-brainex?rebelltitem=2#rebelltitem2\" target=\"_self\"><u>pig brains</u></a> and kept some of them alive for 36 hours. While these revived brains neither were attached to pig bodies nor exhibited the electrical signals associated with consciousness, the study does raise the possibility that such a thing could be done. Other experiments seem to be based more on <em>The Island of Dr. Moreau </em>by H.G. Wells, including <a href=\"https://academic.oup.com/nsr/article/6/3/480/5420749\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><u>one</u></a> in which monkeys were modified to carry a human gene for brain development. These monkeys had better short-term memory and reaction times than non-modified monkeys.</p><h2>Where do we go from here?</h2><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"430\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/4ezS5vQ1j_E\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"730\"></iframe></div><p>The authors do not propose that we stop any particular research but instead consider the problem of moral standing. We should decide now what duties and moral obligations we owe to a sentient creature before the problem is literally looking us in the face.</p><p>While it is true that animal research is tightly regulated, nobody seems to have planned for reanimated pigs or monkeys with human-like intelligence. Though ethics reviews of experiments likely would catch the most egregious experiments before they venture into the realm of Gothic horror, they might miss a few things if we do not engage in some bioethical reflection now.</p><p>The authors suggest that we take two points from Frankenstein to guide us in drawing up new ethical standards: First, we should consider anything we create as existing on a moral plane no matter what the current regulations state. Exactly where a particular creature might fall on the moral spectrum is another question. (For instance, a reanimated pig brain does not have the same moral standing as a human being.) </p><p>Second, they remind us that we must try to avoid holding prejudice toward any moral beings that look or act differently than we do. In the novel, Dr. Frankenstein recoils in horror almost instinctively at what he created with monstrous results (no pun intended). We must be willing to consider atypical beings as potentially worthy of moral standing no matter how strange they may be.</p><p>Finally, they advise that every manipulated organism be treated with respect. This might be the most easily applied — had Victor Frankenstien respected the graves he looted to create his monster, none of the misfortune that followed would have befallen him.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/npW-IJNQzbA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 06 Aug 2021 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDQ3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjcxNzA5Nn0.Db4NBbbUNQznPOqbJm_JJIQYUkOT2w4lq4Cegpau-m8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C180%2C0%2C532&height=700","linkMd5":"ba0b815e4eeb01f511fc936bcb21a4ae","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn11@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-10-085_5161bfa59ca5c9e4.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":192262,"destBytes":192262,"author":"Scotty Hendricks","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDQ3Ny9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NjcxNzA5Nn0.Db4NBbbUNQznPOqbJm_JJIQYUkOT2w4lq4Cegpau-m8/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C180%2C0%2C532&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn11@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-10-085_5161bfa59ca5c9e4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/npW-IJNQzbA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn20@2020_2/2021/08/15/03-53-28-413_7b54ec1431b0a0b9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"How can we fix America's police?","link":"https://bigthink.com/institute-for-humane-studies/how-reform-america-police","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDUxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTgzMzg4NX0.AtXZIzoIXU9XVA-6AespAbk5dNzTFwKZQ2nM4c29VnA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=39%2C0%2C39%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ol><li>Replace consolidated police forces with community policing.</li><li>Encourage discretionary non-enforcement of laws.</li><li>Remove bad financial incentives like civil asset forfeiture.</li></ol><hr/><p>After the murder of George Floyd by police officer Derek Chauvin in May 2020, some<a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/06/12/opinion/sunday/floyd-abolish-defund-police.html\" target=\"_blank\"> Americans argued</a> the country would be better off without police. A police-free zone was<a href=\"https://www.vox.com/identities/2020/6/16/21292723/chaz-seattle-police-free-neighborhood\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> established</a> in Seattle. The zone was<a href=\"https://www.vox.com/policy-and-politics/2020/7/2/21310109/chop-chaz-cleared-violence-explained\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> shut down</a> several months later after four shootings, two deaths, and several sexual assaults.</p><p>Violent crime is an undeniable problem in the United States: homicides<a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/outlook/we-dont-know-why-violent-crime-is-up-but-we-know-theres-more-than-one-cause/2021/07/09/467dd25c-df9a-11eb-ae31-6b7c5c34f0d6_story.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> rose</a> 25 percent from 2019 to 2020 and still<a href=\"https://wtop.com/dc/2021/07/dc-marks-100th-homicide-in-2021/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> continue to rise</a> in some cities. In New York City, voters responded to the recent<a href=\"https://www.huffpost.com/entry/historic-crime-spike-new-york-city-mayor-race_n_60b1adb1e4b0f2a82ee9e363\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> crime surge</a> by nominating former NYPD Captain Eric Adams for mayor. Even in progressive cities, it seems, people still believe policing is the best solution to violent crime. A<a href=\"https://www.npr.org/sections/live-updates-protests-for-racial-justice/2020/07/09/889618702/police-viewed-less-favorably-but-few-want-to-defund-them-survey-finds\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Pew Research Center poll</a> shows that few Americans support abolishing or defunding the police.</p><p>So how can we fix our current policing problems? What would a more humane version of policing look like — and what is stopping us from getting it?</p><p>Here are three police reform ideas from scholars who have studied American policing:</p><h2>1. Replace consolidated police forces with community policing</h2><p>In an<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=WoFkor92dbg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Institute for Humane Studies (IHS) video</a> on community policing, economists Jayme Lemke and Liya Palagashvili trace the history of today's citywide police forces back to Prohibition. Politicians believed consolidating small neighborhood police departments into citywide forces with unified standards would make cities better equipped to enforce federal prohibition laws.</p><p>But consolidation came with a price, Lemke and Palagashvili say. Previously, under smaller neighborhood departments, police officers were seen as long-term partners with the community with the mutual goal of making the neighborhood safer. This is known as community policing, and it incentivizes officers to act in the best interest of the people in their community, which does not always mean arresting offenders. </p><p>\"You have a kid out after curfew,\" Lemke, a senior research fellow at the Mercatus Center, says. \"Does he get hauled home to mom, or does he get hauled to jail?\"</p><div class=\"rm-embed embed-media\"><iframe allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"315\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/WoFkor92dbg\" title=\"YouTube video player\" width=\"560\"></iframe></div><p>But consolidated forces have a one-size-fits-all approach to neighborhood safety: enforce laws through citations and arrests. Police success quickly became measured by numbers: the number of people stopped and arrested. \"The culture is, you're not working unless you are writing summonses or arresting people,\" NYPD Officer Adyl Polanco<a href=\"https://www.npr.org/2015/04/04/395061810/despite-laws-and-lawsuits-quota-based-policing-lingers\" target=\"_blank\"> told</a> NPR.<br/></p><p>In the 1970s, Nobel Prize-winning economist Elinor Ostrom pushed back on the consolidation of police forces and other public departments. Her theory of polycentric governance suggested that communities would be better off with multiple decision-making bodies interacting rather than decision-making power being centralized in larger consolidated bodies. She studied police departments — even riding in the back of police cars — and found that communities with smaller neighborhood police departments had a better relationship with police because that is when coproduction — a shared responsibility for achieving safety — takes place.</p><p>\"Many of the officers in very big departments do not see themselves as responsible to citizens,\" Ostrom<a href=\"https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/journal-of-institutional-economics/article/abs/riding-in-cars-with-boys-elinor-ostroms-adventures-with-the-police/CC1F61D43D67455F26629AC2C431FCEC\" target=\"_blank\"> said</a>. \"They are on duty for specific hours and with an entirely different mentality.\" Ostrom argued that community police forces are more likely to employ officers who are willing to try outside-the-box solutions to solve a neighborhood's unique problems — problems that are rarely fixed through tickets and arrests.</p><h2>2. Encourage discretionary non-enforcement of laws</h2><p>Jake Monaghan, assistant professor of philosophy at the University of New Orleans, spoke about bad policing incentives at a recent IHS symposium, \"<a href=\"https://theihs.org/academic-events/research-workshops-and-symposia/academic-research-seminars/unnecessary-evils-laws-judging-and-policing-in-an-overcriminalized-world/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Unnecessary Evils: Laws, Judging, and Policing in an Overcriminalized World</a>.\" He is currently working on a book about what good policing looks like.</p><p>\"I argue first that one important way to overcome failures in the criminal justice system is to engage in discretionary non-enforcement of certain laws,\" Monaghan writes in his<a href=\"http://jakemonaghan.com/book\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> book abstract</a>. </p><p>Recognizing that discretion is an inevitable element of policing means abandoning any misconception that police should behave like the military. \"Soldiers are typically allowed very little discretion in the course of their work,\" Monaghan<a href=\"http://stockholmcentre.org/police-discretion-and-the-military-analogy/?fbclid=IwAR1SCcW1QwC5FAYaDS8-OGbf66gXfUamme2ikVExQw57DEEdCN3HfG366j4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> writes</a>. \"Policing, especially in the patrol division, is characterized by significant amounts of discretion.\" We should view police officers not as soldiers but \"as genuine professionals who should be exercising discretion by drawing on a body of specialized knowledge.\"</p><p>We can encourage the exercise of legitimate, well-reasoned discretion by specializing the patrol division, Monaghan argues, so that officers responding to calls have the necessary training to decide when <em>not</em> enforcing a law is in the best interest of the community.</p><h2>3. Remove bad financial incentives like civil asset forfeiture</h2><p>Civil asset forfeiture currently allows police departments in most jurisdictions to seize assets from criminal suspects before they are convicted or even arrested. If police claim an asset was involved in a crime, they can confiscate it — then use it to help fund police activities.</p><p>\"Police departments and municipal governments around the country rely on seized assets, whether cash directly or property that can be sold for cash, to help pay for operations and programs that they otherwise could not afford,\" Chris Surprenant, professor of ethics at University of New Orleans, and Jason Brennan, professor of ethics at Georgetown University, write in their book <a href=\"https://www.routledge.com/Injustice-for-All-How-Financial-Incentives-Corrupted-and-Can-Fix-the-US/Surprenant-Brennan/p/book/9781138338821\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Injustice For All: How Financial Incentives Corrupted and Can Fix the Criminal Justice System</em></a>.</p><p>While a police officer cannot lawfully take cash from a suspected drug dealer and deposit into his own bank account, he can use civil asset forfeiture to accomplish essentially the same thing: the more money a police officer seizes, the more money there will be in the police department budget to pay for raises and nicer amenities, Surprenant and Brennan explain.</p><p>Not only does this incentivize police departments to seize more assets from people, but it also incentivizes them to allow some criminal activities to take place so that they can then seize the profits.</p><p>\"For example,\" Surprenant and Brennan explain, \"when disrupting drug trade activities, any illegal drugs seized by law enforcement agents must be destroyed, but they can retain seized cash believed to be connected to drug activity and use it to fund their own operations. That cash can be kept but drugs must be destroyed has led many police departments to establish checkpoints and otherwise run operations to target individuals after sales have been made, rather than preventing those sales from being made in the first place.\"</p><p>States should reform asset forfeiture laws to prevent police departments from enriching themselves through policing, Surprenant and Brennan say. That will help realign police incentives with the long-term safety and well-being of the community.</p><h2>What success looks like</h2><p>As different states and municipalities experiment with police reforms like the three ideas listed above, how can we measure success? What does good policing look like?</p><p><em>The Atlantic</em>'s Conor Friedersdorf<a href=\"https://www.theatlantic.com/national/archive/2014/08/the-9-principles-of-good-policing/378810/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> points</a> to the \"Nine Principles of Policing,\" drafted in 1829 by the London Metropolitan Police Department, as \"the foundations of a civilized law-enforcement agency.\" The fourth principle is: \"To recognize always that the extent to which the cooperation of the public can be secured diminishes proportionately the necessity of the use of physical force and compulsion for achieving police objectives.\"</p><p>This, ultimately, is what we want from good policing: to secure our cooperation as much as possible through peaceful partnership. Successfully doing that means respecting the dignity and diversity of communities, treating individuals humanely, and having no overarching goal other than the peace and safety of the neighborhood. </p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BHKGymbpcSY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 06 Aug 2021 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDUxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTgzMzg4NX0.AtXZIzoIXU9XVA-6AespAbk5dNzTFwKZQ2nM4c29VnA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=39%2C0%2C39%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"d48094f75cda0642e8ee67292ce65a67","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn57@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-25-029_e3b549e6973074ae.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":81926,"destBytes":81926,"author":"Nicole Yeatman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDUxOS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3OTgzMzg4NX0.AtXZIzoIXU9XVA-6AespAbk5dNzTFwKZQ2nM4c29VnA/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=39%2C0%2C39%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn57@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-25-029_e3b549e6973074ae.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BHKGymbpcSY":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn21@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-28-145_a35012fba9f0c9d8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Brain hacks for saving money","link":"https://bigthink.com/personal-growth/how-to-save-money","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2ODcyMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTQ0MTY3MX0.AnZbNbuNfy03L9X5-ucOoAkK6MwkrEIjrl11FTt4W1o/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C521&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>A new episode of \"Your Brain on Money\" explores how our brains evolved systems that make it difficult for us to sacrifice for the future.</li><li>One major reason that it's difficult to save is that we tend to view our future selves as strangers with whom we can't easily connect.</li><li>Saving becomes easier when we learn to connect with our future selves and are mindful of our habits and reward systems.</li></ul><hr/><p><br/></p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube\">\n        <span class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"bd4b1b318870b3bc37441847a8a0ccbe\" style=\"display:block;position:relative;padding-top:56.25%;\"><iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"auto\" lazy-loadable=\"true\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/NfzDSSiLtBQ?rel=0\" style=\"position:absolute;top:0;left:0;width:100%;height:100%;\" width=\"100%\"></iframe></span>\n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">How to trick your brain into saving money</small>\n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">\n            <a href=\"https://youtu.be/NfzDSSiLtBQ\" target=\"_blank\">youtu.be</a>\n        </small>\n        </p><p>You find $100 on the sidewalk. How would you spend it? Out of all the options — order pizza, buy new shoes, take your significant other out to dinner — socking it away into your retirement account is probably the least exciting. Saving can feel like you're throwing money away or giving it to an invisible stranger.<br/></p><p><span></span>There's also a decent chance you don't yet have a retirement account. A <a href=\"https://www.pwc.com/us/en/industries/asset-wealth-management/library/retirement-in-america.html\" target=\"_blank\">survey published in March 2021</a> found that a quarter of U.S. adults have no retirement savings, while many people who are saving aren't saving enough. Of course, that's not necessarily a moral failing. About one in ten U.S. families is living in poverty, and the pandemic brought one of the <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2021-01-25/u-s-suffers-sharpest-rise-in-poverty-rate-in-more-than-50-years\" target=\"_blank\">sharpest ever rises in the national poverty rate</a>.</p><p><span></span>But even people who can afford to save for the future often don't. For example, a <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3158583/\" target=\"_blank\">2011 study</a> involving salaried employees at seven major companies found that roughly 40 percent of workers failed to invest enough in their 401(k) plans so that their employers would match the contributions, meaning they were foregoing free money.</p><p>It is not necessarily wrong to not save a lot of money for the future. But consider the statistics: The median retirement savings account for Americans aged 55 to 64 is <a href=\"https://www.pwc.com/us/en/industries/asset-wealth-management/library/retirement-in-america.html\" target=\"_blank\">estimated</a> to contain about $120,000. If a retiree withdraws money over the next 15 years, that amounts to less than $1,000 per month. That's under the poverty line.</p><p>What about Social Security? Reserves are <a href=\"https://www.gobankingrates.com/retirement/social-security/what-will-social-security-be-in-2035/\" target=\"_blank\">projected to run out by 2035</a>, at which point beneficiaries will start receiving far less than originally expected. The problems associated with depleted Social Security likely will be compounded by the fact that Americans are generally living longer with each generation.</p><p>So, why don't people save more for their retirement? It's partly because people often don't feel like they are saving for <em>their </em>retirement at all. </p><h2>The disconnect between our present and future selves</h2><p>In 50 seconds from now, will you be the same person? What about in 50 days? 50 years? </p><p>As we project our minds into the future, our conception of ourselves becomes more abstract. Sure, most of us would agree that, in a driver's license sense, we will still be the \"same person\" decades from today. But neurological research suggests that is not actually how our brains process this thought experiment.</p><p>A <a href=\"https://static1.squarespace.com/static/528facb6e4b0a18b7e9cde91/t/5314c1c3e4b05444a30d2b45/1393869251983/How+is+our+self+related+to+midline+regions+and+the+default-mode+network.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">large set of brain imaging studies</a> has shown that thinking about ourselves activates a region of the brain called the <a href=\"https://books.google.com/books?hl=en&lr=&id=RpQgBQAAQBAJ&oi=fnd&pg=PA22&dq=cortical+midline+self&ots=il_ihlUghk&sig=4d68_gag74AYNa5JgEKQVis4lHM#v=onepage&q=cortical%20midline%20self&f=false\" target=\"_blank\">medial prefrontal cortex</a> (MPFC). But when we think about other people, the MPFC quiets down and does so even more when we think about others with whom we have little in common. </p><p>Studies have observed that the MPFC also quiets when we think about our future selves. In other words, we see our future selves as strangers with whom it is difficult to emotionally connect. A <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3764505/\" target=\"_blank\">2011 report</a> on how conceptions of the future self transform intertemporal choice noted:</p><p rel=\"margin-left: 20px;\" style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"These findings have important implications for intertemporal choice. If people tend to consider the future self as a stranger — that is, if that distant future self feels on an emotional level like another person — then they may rationally have no more reason to save their money for their future selves than to give that money to a stranger.\"</p><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"d81ae1d2fd73d0852427a9e5752a80ef\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"8d1ff\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2ODczMC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4OTQ3ODgwNH0.qMfIVZKT0-lz_ZsMal_Kf3-VXoMLX7hVRh1qQQ3vGiE/img.png?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Future self-continuity scale that has been used to assess similarity between current and future selves.</small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"><a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3764505/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Hershfield</a>, PudMed Central, 2013</small></p><p>The inability to connect with our future selves can contribute to a phenomenon called temporal discounting, in which we care less about future outcomes than immediate ones. A <a href=\"https://uccs.edu/Documents/ktomlin/Pronin,%20Olivola,%20Kennedy;%202008.pdf\" target=\"_blank\">2008 study</a> explored temporal discounting by asking students to drink a cup of a disgusting liquid for the good of science. Some students signed up to drink it that day, others to do so months later.<br/></p><p>The group that agreed to drink the liquid that day chose to drink an average of a couple tablespoons. But the months-later group signed themselves up to down a half cup. Just like procrastinating work or racking up a credit card bill, it is psychologically and emotionally easier to dump today's problems on our future selves.</p><h2>The brain in conflict: reward, habit, thinking</h2><p>Like the self, the brain isn't a single entity with a singular goal but rather a collection of interacting systems that often conflict with each other. Three broad systems are particularly relevant in terms of planning for the future: reward, habit, and thinking. </p><p>These systems evolved over time to serve useful functions. For example, our reward system nudges us to focus on things we need, like food and social connections. Our habits help us perform behaviors — some productive, some not so much — on autopilot, rather than having to deliberate each one. And thinking helps us solve complex problems and plan for the future.</p><p>Alex Korb, a neuroscientist, author, and adjunct assistant professor at UCLA, told Big Think that it's useful to conceptualize these different brain regions as being like different types of friends:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"Your prefrontal cortex, the thinking part of the brain, is saying, 'Hey let's do it this way because that's going to get us to where we're trying to go.' And then the habit part of the brain says, 'Well, no, let's do it this way because that's the way we've always done it, and that's more comfortable.' And the reward center of the brain says, 'Oh look, there's a cookie!'\" </p><p>When it comes to saving for retirement, it's not a fair fight among these three systems. Our habit and reward systems have the upper hand because they pull us away from long-term planning and toward immediate gratification. After all, it's relatively easy to keep doing things the way we have always done them, and it is hard to resist immediate rewards. </p><p>Interrupting these systems takes willpower. But willpower alone often isn't enough to do the trick. Some psychologists think that is because we have a limited amount of willpower that can run out after we become too stressed. This idea, called <a href=\"https://hbr.org/2016/11/have-we-been-thinking-about-willpower-the-wrong-way-for-30-years\" target=\"_blank\">ego depletion</a>, makes sense in terms of saving for retirement, an activity that's not only financially stressful but also makes us contemplate existential issues like mortality, aging, and purpose. </p><p>The depletion of willpower can affect how we perceive rewards. Psychologists Janet Metcalfe and Walter Mischel — Mischel conducted the famous <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_marshmallow_experiment\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Stanford marshmallow experiment</a> on delaying gratification — proposed that we view rewards with two systems: hot and cool. The American Psychological Association <a href=\"https://www.apa.org/topics/personality/willpower\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">writes</a>:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"The cool system is cognitive in nature. It's essentially a thinking system, incorporating knowledge about sensations, feelings, actions and goals — reminding yourself, for instance, why you shouldn't eat the marshmallow. While the cool system is reflective, the hot system is impulsive and emotional. The hot system is responsible for quick, reflexive responses to certain triggers — such as popping the marshmallow into your mouth without thinking of the long-term implications. If this framework were a cartoon, the cool system would be the angel on your shoulder, and the hot system the devil.\"</p><p>When we choose immediate gratification through the hot system, we not only miss out on long-term rewards but also reinforce our bad habits. So, how can we use the cool system to disrupt our habits and plan for the future?</p><h2>How to trick your brain into saving for retirement</h2><p>Odds are that socking away money into a retirement account is never going to feel as exciting as splurging on a night out. But there are ways to make it easier. </p><p>One is to try to emotionally and psychologically connect with your future self. For example, there are applications where you can upload a photo of yourself and see computer-generated images of what you might look like decades down the road. The exercise seems to help people view their relationships with their future selves as less abstract. </p><p>A <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B004CFA9WI/ref=dp-kindle-redirect?_encoding=UTF8&btkr=1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">2011 experiment</a> explored this concept by having two groups of participants view themselves in virtual reality. One group saw a virtual representation of their current selves, while another was shown an age-progressed virtual version of themselves. Afterward, both groups were asked to allocate hypothetical money toward various things, including a retirement account. The group that came face to face with their older selves saved more money.</p><p>You do not need virtual reality to start saving. One of the easiest ways is to choose an amount of money to save every month — a reasonable amount that won't cause too much stress — and then set up automatic deposits into a retirement account. The set-it-and-forget-it strategy utilizes the cool system while removing the \"hot system\" temptations we face when choosing how to spend a lump sum of money.</p><p>Ultimately, it's not really important how you save, but that you start saving at all. That is partly because compound interest yields higher returns if you start saving early. But it is also because consistently saving money can retrain your brain into thinking saving is important; after all, if you keep procrastinating saving, your brain will learn that it must not be worthwhile.</p><p>\"One of the keys to being sustainably happy is really just understanding yourself better so that you can make choices that are best for you and your circumstances and understanding your brain can be a big part of that,\" Korb told Big Think. \"Saving one dollar a day is infinitely better than saving zero dollars per day. So just take one little step in the next direction.\"</p><div></div><ul></ul><div></div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BvcdMrZo_6A\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\"
