Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2ODcyMS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY3MTQ0MTY3MX0.AnZbNbuNfy03L9X5-ucOoAkK6MwkrEIjrl11FTt4W1o/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C521&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn13@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-09-132_1ef047361f43d58f.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE2ODczMC9vcmlnaW4ucG5nIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4OTQ3ODgwNH0.qMfIVZKT0-lz_ZsMal_Kf3-VXoMLX7hVRh1qQQ3vGiE/img.png?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn83@2020_6/2021/08/15/03-53-28-272_8d1ff3a97ce61ce8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/BvcdMrZo_6A":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn40@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-983_1ed8a14ad99d6d4c.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"How to reduce gun violence without taking people’s guns","link":"https://bigthink.com/Northwell-Health/how-to-reduce-gun-violence","description":"<iframe frameborder=\"0\" height=\"100%\" scrolling=\"no\" src=\"https://www.rebelmouse.com/res/scraper/embed/?jwplayer_video_url=https%3A%2F%2Fcontent.jwplatform.com%2Fplayers%2Fe5qH6gFy-FvQKszTI.js\" width=\"100%\"></iframe><br/><ul><li>Approximately 41,000 people are killed each year due to gun violence. That's more lives lost to guns than to car accidents. So why do we devote more attention (and money) to car safety than we do gun safety?<br/>As Northwell Health CEO Michael Dowling points out, the deaths are not the whole story. The physical, emotional, and psychological trauma reverberates through communities and the public at-large. \"This is not just not about guns,\" says Dowling,\" this is a serious public health issue and we've got to look at it that way.</li><li>Hospitals often deal with the aftermath of gun violence, but they can play a key role in preventing it. Medical staff are trained to assess health risk factors. Dowling argues that a similar approach is needed for guns. \"We have to be much more holistic in our approach.\"<br/>Interested in getting involved? On December 15th, Northwell Health is hosting a hybrid virtual / in-person Gun </li><li>Violence Prevention Forum, where participants can engage around the shared goal of raising their collective voices and catalyzing action to enact needed systems change. Learn more and register for the public forum here: <a href=\"https://preventgunviolence.com/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">https://preventgunviolence.com/</a></li></ul><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/cVZwX-eFm6g\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Aug 2021 17:30:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"","linkMd5":"63a011b6e6521c4cbe1c08c509d208df","bgimgJsdelivr":"","metaImg":"","author":"Michael Dowling","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/cVZwX-eFm6g":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn31@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-306_4bcfc3314aaa4a6a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Science in the court: how neuroimaging can improve the “reasonable person” standard","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/reasonable-person-standard","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMzM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDMyMzE5OX0.UCNy3EygeClRktKSDSUvCXiIcjPpvRTjTaMuXMOzzMs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul> <li>The reasonable person is a so-called legal fiction that refers to \"an ordinary person who exercises care while avoiding extremes of boldness and carefulness.\"</li><li>A new study explored how fMRI might be used to minimize subjectivity and bias in trademark law.</li><li>Trademark law is uniquely suited for neuroimaging because it often involves questions over visual similarity, which is relatively easy to measure through fMRI.</li></ul><hr/><p>How closely does the packaging of Toffee Crisp resemble Reese's peanut butter cups?</p><p>Too close, according to Hershey. In 2014, the chocolate manufacturer, which owns the Reese's brand, filed a trademark lawsuit against an American candy company that was importing Toffee Crisp from the UK, arguing that it had no right to sell Toffee Crisp in the U.S. because the product's black and orange packaging looked too much like Reese's. </p><h2>A neuroscientifically informed \"reasonable person\" test</h2><p>How can courts resolve these types of ambiguous questions? One way is for courts to seek insight from a hypothetical character: the \"reasonable person.\" Since the 19th century, the reasonable person has served as a standard for how an ordinary adult can be expected to act in situations disputed in civil and criminal courts, ranging from cases involving negligence and crime to free speech and intellectual property. <em>Black's Law Dictionary</em> defines the term as \"an ordinary person who exercises care while avoiding extremes of boldness and carefulness.\" </p><p>It is a murky definition whose application is subjective. In trademark law, determining whether a logo is too similar to another often comes down to Justice Potter Stewart's iconic expression, \"I know it when I see it.\" And more broadly, the reasonable person standard has been criticized as being vulnerable to judges' and jurors' explicit and implicit biases. </p><p>A new paper published on the preprint server <a href=\"https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=3876774\" target=\"_blank\">SSRN</a> proposes that brain imaging technologies can bring more objectivity to the reasonable person standard, starting with trademark law. </p><h2>The neuroscience of trademarks</h2><p>Trademark law is uniquely suited for neuroimaging because it centers on questions over visual similarity. That is partly because the brain constructs mental representations of visual information in a relatively clearly defined way; for example, showing people an image of the Nike logo several times almost surely will generate more consistent brain activity than having them repeatedly imagine, say, a hypothetical crime. </p><p>The researchers noted:</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\"...there is broad consensus on the construct validity and neuroanatomical substrates of different visual processes. Substantial evidence from both lesion and neuroimaging studies indicates that regions within the fusiform and inferotemporal cortices [brain regions associated with face and object recognition] engage in holistic, as opposed to parts-based, representation of objects.\"</p><p>The researchers aimed to construct a reliable index of visual similarity — a scale that uses neural responses to rank how closely a set of products resembles the product in question, called the \"reference product.\" Under fMRI, the participants viewed a rapid presentation of product images: the reference product, four fake products, and four real products with varying degrees of visual similarity.</p><p>To reliably measure visual similarity, the researchers used a technique called fMRI repetition suppression, which exploits the fact that our neural response to a stimulus declines upon repeated exposure.</p><p>\"Critically for our current purposes, substantial evidence indicates that the relative suppression between two distinct stimuli can be used to assess the degree of overlap in neural representations of these stimuli,\" the researchers wrote. \"Thus, by repeatedly presenting the likeness of different products, we can construct an index of visual similarity using neural responses from object sensitive regions of the visual system identified a priori.\"</p><p>To ensure that other factors did not confound the results, the study was designed such that the participants did not know the goal of the study as they underwent fMRI. After the fMRI scan, the participants were asked to rate the visual similarity of the nine products to the reference products. The fMRI results strongly correlated with the subjective ratings. </p><p>The second part of the study was conducted to measure bias within subjective surveys of visual similarity. The researchers asked 870 people to complete an online survey in which they rated the visual similarity of the ten products used in the first experiment. The respondents were shown one of three surveys. One used wording that favored a hypothetical plaintiff, referring to trademark infringing products as \"copycats.\" Another was worded in favor of the defendant. The third was designed neutrally.</p><p>The fMRI data correlated strongly with the responses from the neutral survey, \"suggesting that the neural similarity index is indeed capable of distinguishing between surveys containing different amounts of bias,\" the researchers wrote. </p><h2>Removing bias from the reasonable person standard</h2><p>Still, the study had some limitations in terms of its applicability to the legal system. For one, the approach cannot objectively show that one product is \"too similar\" to another; it can only offer degrees of similarity. Another is generalizability. The researchers noted that they do not claim or anticipate that this method will have useful applications outside of intellectual property law.</p><p>Neuroimaging methods are unlikely to replace other forms of evidence used in courts, namely surveys. More likely, they will provide supplementary evidence. But the approach could be developed into a tool that can detect bias and boost confidence in surveys, a form of legal evidence that's unique for its ability to gauge public opinion on a large scale.</p><p>\"Although of course still highly imperfect and incomplete, even the small step we take here constitutes a productive and meaningful advance, given the ubiquity of, and acknowledged flaws in, the reasonable person test,\" the researchers concluded.</p><div></div><ul></ul><div></div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/hH8xFLuzTbs\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Fri, 13 Aug 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMzM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDMyMzE5OX0.UCNy3EygeClRktKSDSUvCXiIcjPpvRTjTaMuXMOzzMs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700","linkMd5":"467f713511462a18140a6266f0b44098","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn54@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-259_ddacb67572e3a38d.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":109068,"destBytes":109068,"author":"Stephen Johnson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzIxMzM3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY4MDMyMzE5OX0.UCNy3EygeClRktKSDSUvCXiIcjPpvRTjTaMuXMOzzMs/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C1%2C0&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn54@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-259_ddacb67572e3a38d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/hH8xFLuzTbs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn15@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-347_ec4746fca6b7017a.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Robots may be more like animals than humans","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/robots-more-like-animals-humans","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDc1My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NDA1NDM0M30.f7WIsxXC0X7EDi-pPQ7_4d9hI4QIqpevG7SVDYkbncY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C182%2C0%2C201&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p><em>This <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/technology/kate-darling\" target=\"_blank\">article</a> was originally published on our sister site, Freethink.</em></p><p>We're nearly a quarter into the 21st century, and by now, the Terminator-style portrayal of robots taking over the world has become a tired cliche. While it's seductive, most of us are aware that this isn't (likely) the future of intelligent life. But what will that look like, then?</p><hr/><p>According to Kate Darling, a robot ethicist at MIT and author of \"<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/New-Breed-History-Animals-Reveals/dp/1250296102\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">The New Breed: What Our History With Animals Reveals About Our Future with Robots</a>,\" the answer is right in front of us: animals.</p><p>While we have traditionally viewed robots as human-like, Darling believes the more apt comparison is seeing them as a different kind of \"animal.\"</p><blockquote>When we expect a robot to behave like a human, it's a very disappointing experience.<br/><cite><br/>KATE DARLING</cite><br/></blockquote><p>Robots will increasingly occupy shared spaces with humans, <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/articles/sophia-the-robot\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">social robots</a> will take off, and the questions around how humans should treat and interact with robots has never been more critical, Darling argues.</p>\n        <p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"ccfcf532105ac97c4cf86bef92ca809b\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"7e431\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDc0OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MzgxMzkyM30.TtbJ6Z8aQG6AGh1wLO_kSS0nFGyed4tswMQNsPAswyM/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-caption\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Caption...\">Kate Darling, author and robot ethicist at MIT. </small><small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit: Kate Darling</small></p>\n        <p>Her point isn't that robots and animals are the same or that they should be used exactly the same way, but that we should be open to the different ways we can collaborate with robots, harnessing their diverse range of skills and abilities — as we do with animals.<br/></p><p>I spoke to Darling about how a robot's design affects our interaction with it, why we should stop worrying about <a href=\"https://www.freethink.com/shows/wrong/season-2/will-robots-take-our-jobs\" target=\"_blank\">robots replacing humans</a>, and more. Here is our conversation, edited and condensed for clarity.</p><p><strong>Why have robots traditionally been designed to look like humans? What is the thinking behind that?</strong></p><p>We've always been fascinated with recreating ourselves. We had automata back in ancient times that were recreations of human bodies that could move around. Even the earliest artificial intelligence researchers started out with a goal of recreating human intelligence.</p><p>With robots and AI, in particular, they are machines that can sense and think and make autonomous decisions and learn. So we tend to automatically compare them to ourselves as well because of our inherent tendency to compare everything to ourselves. And traditionally, a lot of robots have been human-shaped — even though that's not necessarily the most practical form.</p><p><strong>What are the problems with this human-like design?</strong></p><p>So there's this subconscious comparison of robots to humans that has been enhanced by the design. I think it doesn't make sense. First of all, AI is not like human intelligence — robots don't have the same skills as people. So oftentimes when we expect a robot to behave like a human, it's a very disappointing experience. That's not to say that robots and AI aren't smart, just that they have a very different type of intelligence and skill than people do.</p><blockquote>Our question shouldn't be, 'at what point can we recreate human ability and human skill in a robot?' The question is, 'why would we want to do that in the first place when we can create something different?'<br/><cite><br/>KATE DARLING</cite><br/></blockquote><p>Also, this comparison really limits us. The early AI researchers were trying to recreate a human brain and human intelligence, but that's not where we've ended up. And so our question shouldn't be, \"at what point can we recreate human ability and human skill in a robot?\" The question is, \"why would we want to do that in the first place when we can create something different?\" Robots and AI don't think or behave like us, but they are very useful and very smart.</p><p><strong>Instead, you suggest using animals as a way to think about robots. What are the parallels here?</strong></p><p>There are so many fun parallels. For thousands of years, we've used animals as a supplement to human ability. Not because they do what we do, but because their skill sets are so different from ours.</p><p>We used oxen to plow our fields, we've used horses to let us travel around in new ways. In some ways, a horse-drawn carriage is the original semi-autonomous vehicle. We've used pigeons to carry mail or deliver medicine in ways similar to how we're using drones today. We used them to take aerial photographs. So they were the original hobby photography drone. We've used dolphins in the Navy to detect mines underwater or locate lost underwater equipment, which is a similar function to how we're starting to use underwater robots today.</p><p><strong>But animals have feelings, and robots don't. How does this affect the way we do, or should, treat robots?<br/></strong><br/>Right. So this is something that has always really fascinated me about human-robot interaction. What it actually says about how we treat other entities. Because in many cases we have not treated animals very well in partnering with them. And in fact, in Western society, we're often quite hypocritical about how we think about how we want to treat other beings and how we actually treat them.</p><p>So a lot of us think that we care about whether other beings feel or whether they have intelligence or whether they can suffer, but if you look at the history of animal rights in Western society, it quickly becomes apparent that we have only protected the animals that are cute or that we care about culturally, or that we have some emotional relationship to.</p><p>What's so interesting about human-robot interaction research is it's showing that we treat robots in very similar ways, where we treat some of them that we have no emotional connection to as tools and products, and then others we treat as companions or develop emotional attachments to.</p><p>So it's entirely possible that if we don't stop and think about this, that we may default to caring more about a robot that feels nothing than we might about a slimy slug in our backyard. It's actually a unique moment in time where we could stop and think and maybe nudge our behavior in a way that's more consistent with what we feel our values are.</p><p><strong>It's interesting you say that because I was thinking the opposite — that we might treat animals kindly, but we sometimes treat robots (especially social ones) with detachment. And there can be harmful effects of this. For instance, if we talk \"down\" to an Amazon Alexa, it has implications for the way we might treat women in our lives.</strong></p><p>So I do think there actually is possibly an argument for treating technology with some kindness, as ridiculous as that sounds. Even though the technology can't feel and we're not anywhere close to having sentient robots or robot consciousness.</p><blockquote>We're seeing robots being designed in a very lifelike way, certain robots, and robots that can respond to being kicked, for example, with a simulation of pain. And one question is, even though the robot can't feel, should we let people kick them?<br/><cite><br/>KATE DARLING</cite><br/></blockquote><p>But there's questions around our own behavior. So if you get used to barking commands at Alexa, or your kid gets used to barking commands at Alexa, you could get used to barking commands at women or women named Alexa or other people. Parents have raised enough concern about this that a lot of these home voice assistant companies have released features to turn on a magic word feature so that it makes Alexa only respond if you say \"please\" and \"thank you,\" for example.</p><p>But then you get into all sorts of questions with different designs of robots. Increasingly we're seeing robots being designed in a very lifelike way, certain robots, and robots that can respond to being kicked, for example, with a simulation of pain. And one question is, even though the robot can't feel, should we let people kick them?</p><p>And what if we had a real-life Westworld theme park, where people could go and do anything they want to life-like robots? Is that a healthy outlet for violent behavior or (does it) train people's cruelty muscles? I don't have an answer to the question, but it is a question that is going to be raised very soon.</p><p><strong>Right. So on the flip side, maybe we could go in the other direction and make them seem less lifelike at all and more like just a neutral object that we don't associate with any kind of life?</strong></p><p>We can try. What we're also seeing in the research is that it's really hard to turn off this tendency that we have to treat robots like living things. Even something as simple as the Roomba vacuum cleaner — just because it's moving around on its own, people will name the Roomba. People will feel bad for the Roomba when it gets stuck. So it's a very difficult human tendency to counteract.</p><p>And in fact, a lot of animal researchers and nature researchers have moved away from the idea that we have to get rid of how we project ourselves onto animals and have said, \"Okay, this is something that is there, we just need to be very aware of it, and we can nudge our behavior in certain directions, but we're not going to get rid of the tendency entirely.\"</p><p>And maybe that's a good thing because it means that we can relate to animals in certain ways that might be actually beneficial for humans. So having therapy dogs or having pets as companionship can actually be a very positive thing for people.</p><p>Or military robots, where soldiers are becoming emotionally attached to the bomb disposal units that they work with. Which, at first blush, you would say, \"Okay, that's terrible. We don't want soldiers to be risking their lives or behaving in an inefficient way on a battlefield because they've developed an emotional connection to a robot.\"</p><p>But at the same time, if you look at the history of the role that animals have played in war, yes, soldiers sometimes made bad decisions based on wanting to save their dog or their horse on the battlefield. But the animals brought so much emotional comfort to soldiers in very stressful situations that it's not clear to me that it's necessarily a bad thing, even if we could prevent it.</p><p><strong>Many people fear that robots are going to threaten us in some way, or replace us. How does shifting it to a view of an animal change the way that we look at that issue?</strong></p><p>Particularly in Western society, we have this idea that there's this constant threat of robots rising up against us or coming to replace us. And in part that comes from this comparison of robots to humans — and it's very limiting. It influences a lot of our conversations from what intelligence is, to our conversations about robots and jobs and robots replacing people one-to-one.</p><p>Using the animal analogy helps us step away from this fear of being replaced. And the animals obviously have not done that. Animals have disrupted society. They have created completely different workplaces for people. They have revolutionized farming and transportation and all sorts of things that technology is also going to disrupt — but it's not the same type of fear that we've had with animals, about animals rising up against us.</p><p>Fear with robots is also quite misplaced, given that we're not anywhere close to having artificial superintelligence or any type of science-fictional scenario that gets a lot of attention in the press — it's actually the wrong question to be worried about.</p><p><strong>What's been the driving force behind your research? The question you are most interested in?</strong></p><p>The thing that blows my mind is our tendency to treat robots like they're alive, even if we know perfectly well that they're just machines. Just a few weeks ago I got this baby harp seal robot called a PARO. It's a medical device that's used with dementia patients in a nursing home. And it looks like a very cute baby seal. It doesn't do very much, it just kind of responds to touch. Makes these little movements and little sounds. I was showing it off to the group of roboticists that I work with. They create social robots — they specifically design robots that give off cues like this.</p><p>They were all like, \"Oh, it's so cute. Oh, look, it's doing XYZ!\" So even the people who build the programs are not immune. In fact, still very susceptible to being swayed by these artificial cues that we've programmed into these machines. It seems to be such a deep biological tendency that we have. It always surprises me, even though I've seen it happen and there's so much research on it.</p><p>I think that we're not talking about it enough and not acknowledging enough of the incredible social tendency that we have that is going to impact how we integrate these machines because we treat them so differently than other devices.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Co46JpNckXA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Sat, 07 Aug 2021 16:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDc1My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NDA1NDM0M30.f7WIsxXC0X7EDi-pPQ7_4d9hI4QIqpevG7SVDYkbncY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C182%2C0%2C201&height=700","linkMd5":"c9a01a6d2bfe2874b219ca59ca5f47ef","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn17@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-134_c76870c1ac7d4e65.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":21470,"destBytes":21470,"author":"Hope Reese","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDc1My9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NDA1NDM0M30.f7WIsxXC0X7EDi-pPQ7_4d9hI4QIqpevG7SVDYkbncY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C182%2C0%2C201&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn17@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-27-134_c76870c1ac7d4e65.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0NDc0OS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MzgxMzkyM30.TtbJ6Z8aQG6AGh1wLO_kSS0nFGyed4tswMQNsPAswyM/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn28@2020_4/2021/08/15/03-53-28-009_7e43108be2c9b317.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/Co46JpNckXA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn35@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-28-334_c0d8c4bb8db9f475.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588789},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Meet the world’s first ‘digital species’ for conservation","link":"https://bigthink.com/technology-innovation/nft-bird","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MDg1Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTk1MDAzMX0.mLr_3cY8tnGmGzhMhYjxmnpmd9Ngo4bnEG5bTlamTZU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C267%2C0%2C-1&height=700\"/><br/><br/><p>The Seychelles magpie-robin is up for sale as a digital nature collectible in an innovative campaign to raise funds for conservation efforts to save the endangered bird.</p><hr/><p>The <a href=\"https://exchange.iucngreenlist.org/marketplace/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">world's first digital species</a> was launched by Nature Seychelles, supported by its international partners, the International Union for Conservation for Nature and the Porini Foundation.</p><p>The bird is being offered as a limited-edition non-fungible token (NFT) – a one-of-a-kind piece of digital content linked to an image or video – meaning that only 59 'digital twins' of the endangered species will be released for purchase.</p><p>\"The Seychelles magpie-robin will take us into the uncharted waters of digital tokens for conservation, with a limited edition of the first 59 collectables – representing a 'digital twin of this endangered living bird,\"<a href=\"http://natureseychelles.org/knowledge-centre/news-and-stories/834-press-release-the-seychelles-magpie-robin-becomes-the-world-s-first-digital-species-and-it-s-for-sale\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> said Nature Seychelles CEO Dr Nirmal Shah</a>.</p><p>\"As traditional sources of funding have dried up, we need innovative ways to support long-term programmes that are the backbone of successful conservation. This is just the beginning of experiments to find new income streams for conservation.\"</p><h3>Seychelles magpie-robin listed as Endangered</h3><p>The Seychelles magpie-robin, or Pi-Santez in Creole, is a glossy black and white bird with a black bill and long tail that lives on the islands of Fregate, Cousin, Cousine, Denis and Aride.</p><p>The creature, whose scientific name is Copsychus sechellarum, is listed as Endangered due to its low population of<a href=\"http://www.natureseychelles.org/what-we-do/54-what-we-do/conservation-networks/80-smart-seychelles-magpie-robin-recovery-team\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> about 460 birds</a> on the Indian Ocean archipelago, with<a href=\"https://edition.cnn.com/videos/world/2021/07/11/seychelles-magpie-nft-spc-intl.cnn\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> 59 on the Cousin Island Special Reserve</a>.</p><p>However, the Seychelles magpie-robin was<a href=\"http://www.natureseychelles.org/knowledge-centre/seychelles-wildlife-2/endemic-bird-species/46-seychelles-magpie-robin\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> downgraded from Critically Endangered in 2005</a> as numbers increased following intensive conservation efforts.</p><h3>Conservation saw bird's population grow</h3><p>Much of this population growth is due to the Seychelles Magpie Robin Recovery Team, which was set up in 1990 when there were<a href=\"http://www.natureseychelles.org/what-we-do/54-what-we-do/conservation-networks/80-smart-seychelles-magpie-robin-recovery-team\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> only 23 birds on one island</a>.</p><p>The early days of the programme, which was first run by Birdlife and the Royal Society for Protection of Birds before being passed to Nature Seychelles in 1998, was focused on stabilizing and recovering the last population on Fregate Island.</p><p>Once numbers stabilized and increased, the Seychelles magpie-robin population was re-established on the islands of Cousin, Cousine and Aride.</p><p>Since 2004, all islands that are home to the birds have taken overall management responsibility for their own populations, and decisions are agreed by a local team of stakeholders along with the Ministry of Environment and Nature Seychelles.</p><h3>NFTs to boost conservation funds post-pandemic</h3><p>Nature Seychelles, like other conservation groups, saw usual sources of income such as tourism dry up during the pandemic, leading them to turn to blockchain to boost fundraising.</p><p>Each of the Seychelles magpie-robin NFTs – whose authenticity is confirmed by blockchain, meaning it can't be duplicated or manipulated – is a representation of a living bird currently protected on the Seychelles' Cousin Island Special Reserve.</p><p>They can be purchased for as <a href=\"https://www.africaninsider.com/technology/seychelles-sells-digital-endangered-bird/\" target=\"_blank\">little as $10 or up to $10,000</a>, with the organization raising<a href=\"https://www.france24.com/en/live-news/20210720-seychelles-sells-digital-endangered-bird\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> $2,500 on the first day of sale alone</a>.</p><p>\"You can contribute whatever you want, and the money will go directly to us to help manage this species,\"<a href=\"https://www.france24.com/en/live-news/20210720-seychelles-sells-digital-endangered-bird\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Shah told AFP</a>. \"This is just the beginning of experiments to find new income streams for conservation.\"</p><h3>Conservation vital for Earth's biodiversity</h3><p>Conserving species of all kinds is vital in maintaining the Earth's biodiversity, which is the variety of<a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/news/2018/mar/12/what-is-biodiversity-and-why-does-it-matter-to-us\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> life on the planet in all of its forms and interactions</a>.</p><p>Each of these species and organisms work together to maintain balance and support life, so a rich biodiversity indicates a healthy ecosystem – which is beneficial both for the environment and humankind.</p><p>One million species are currently thought to be at risk of extinction with <a href=\"https://www.un.org/sustainabledevelopment/blog/2019/05/nature-decline-unprecedented-report/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">nature declining at rates unprecedented in history</a>, according to a 2019 UN report.</p><p>Reprinted with permission of the World Economic Forum. Read the <a href=\"https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2021/07/seychelles-conservation-digital-token/\" target=\"_blank\">original article</a>.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/G6-mr1K3Frs\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Tue, 10 Aug 2021 19:54:53 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MDg1Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTk1MDAzMX0.mLr_3cY8tnGmGzhMhYjxmnpmd9Ngo4bnEG5bTlamTZU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C267%2C0%2C-1&height=700","linkMd5":"53388d1620ac6a1706935359549503db","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn28@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-042_cf2676de6751a03a.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":700,"sourceBytes":55852,"destBytes":55852,"author":"Natalie Marchant","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE3MDg1Mi9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY0NTk1MDAzMX0.mLr_3cY8tnGmGzhMhYjxmnpmd9Ngo4bnEG5bTlamTZU/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C267%2C0%2C-1&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn28@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-23-042_cf2676de6751a03a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/G6-mr1K3Frs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn19@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-146_411ac11dc274bcc8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588780},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"Why the U.S. is trapped in \"endless war\"","link":"https://bigthink.com/politics-current-affairs/us-united-states-endless-war","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODM0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NDUxMTg1MH0.U0oUArcjhN2ttwJVrcecvjgwc8sXhvYdHh6H2zb-4TY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C18%2C0%2C184&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>There is a relationship between establishing foreign military bases and the incidence of war.</li><li>Unless the U.S. completely withdraws from the Middle East, we will continue fighting there.</li><li>Oftentimes, wars — such as the war on terrorism — fail to achieve our geopolitical objectives.</li></ul><hr/><p>Nearly a decade ago, around the height of U.S. involvement in the war in Afghanistan, I witnessed the arrival of amputees and other badly wounded military personnel arriving at Landstuhl Regional Medical Center in Germany. A trauma center comparable to elite U.S. hospi­tals, Landstuhl is the main U.S. military hospital in Europe and has received thousands of casualties from Afghanistan, Iraq, and other war zones over the last 20 years of continuous U.S. warfare.</p><p>Curiously, as the U.S. military withdraws from Afghanistan and has only a few thousand troops in Iraq and Syria, the Pentagon now is spending <a href=\"https://www.stripes.com/theaters/europe/349-million-in-projects-continue-even-as-military-prepares-to-draw-down-in-germany-1.640387\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">$1 billion</a> building a new military hospital in Germany to replace Landstuhl. After my visit to Landstuhl, I asked former <em>Washington Post </em>Pulitzer Prize-winning military reporter Walter Pincus what we should make of spending $1 billion to replace this world-class medical facility.</p><p>Without missing a beat Pincus said, \"It implies we're going to keep fighting.\"</p><h2>More military bases mean more war</h2><p>Beyond the new hospital in Germany, there are many signs that despite the Afghanistan withdrawal, the U.S. military is going to keep fighting. Above all, dozens of U.S. military bases will remain in nearly every country in the Greater Middle East. These bases have played key roles in launching U.S. wars in Afghanistan, Iraq, Somalia, Libya, Syria, Yemen, and at least 19 other countries since 2001. An examination of two decades of war and the longer history of U.S. wars since independence suggests that unless U.S. leaders shut down more of this military infrastructure, the country will continue to fight \"endless wars\" far beyond Afghanistan, resulting in more amputees and other wounded and dead arriving in Germany.</p><p>Since World War II, the United States has maintained thousands of bases in foreign lands — a larger collection of foreign bases than any country, people, or empire in world history. Today the military controls around 750 military bases in some 80 countries and colonies outside the 50 states and Washington, DC. The rest of the world's militaries combined likely control <a href=\"http://overseasbases.net/fact-sheet.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">fewer than 250</a> foreign bases; China has just five, plus bases in Tibet.</p><div class=\"rm-shortcode amazon-assets-widget\" contenteditable=\"false\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"3feb637c51021f17156a56031fadc307\">\n    <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/0520300874?tag=bigthink00-20&linkCode=ogi&th=1&psc=1\" target=\"_blank\">\n        <img class=\"amazon-assets-widget__image widget__image\" data-runner-src=\"https://m.media-amazon.com/images/I/51LCNC1WiML._SL500_.jpg\" type=\"lazy-image\"/>\n        <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__description\">\n            <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__title\" style=\"display: block;\">The United States of War: A Global History of America's Endless Conflicts, from Columbus to the Islamic State (Volume 48) (California Series in Public Anthropology)</div>\n            <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__by-amazon\"><!-- <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/0520300874?tag=bigthink00-20&linkCode=ogi&th=1&psc=1\" target=\"_blank\">by now at amazone</a> --></div>\n            <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__list-price\"><span class=\"grey\">List Price: </span><span class=\"list-price\">$21.49</span></div>\n            <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__new-price\"><span class=\"grey\">New From: </span><span class=\"new-price\">$15.99</span> <span class=\"grey\">in Stock</span></div>\n            <div class=\"amazon-assets-widget__used-price\"><span class=\"grey\">Used From: </span><span class=\"used-price\">$15.98</span> <span class=\"grey\">in Stock</span></div>\n        </div>\n    </a>\n</div>\n<p>An unprecedented collection of U.S. bases began to dot the Middle East shortly after the Soviet Union invaded Afghanistan in 1979. These bases as well as installations in Germany and other parts of Europe and as far away as Thailand and Japan have enabled permanent war since 2001, and a longer series of wars and invasions since the 1980s. In Qatar and Bahrain, the Air Force and Navy have built large regional headquarters. In Kuwait, the Army has had a major presence since the 1991 Gulf War, totaling around eleven base sites now. There are 13 base sites in Turkey, six in Oman, three in the United Arab Emirates, two in both Jordan and Djibouti, as well as a presence in Egypt. The military maintains acknowledged and secretive installations in both Saudi Arabia and Israel, numbering eleven and eight respectively. In Iraq, the military still operates from around six installations. Four more are in Syria. In Afghanistan, after the official withdrawal, 650 troops will remain between the U.S. embassy and Kabul International Airport; U.S. special forces, military contractors, and the CIA almost certainly will continue to operate in the country.<br/></p><p>Other U.S. bases are nearby in Greece (eight sites), Somalia (five), Cyprus, Tunisia, Georgia, Kosovo, and on the Indian Ocean island Diego Garcia. The Navy has at least one aircraft carrier — a floating base — on an effectively permanent basis in the Persian/Arabian Gulf. Despite this massive infrastructure, the Biden administration recently has explored creating <a href=\"https://www.seattletimes.com/nation-world/nation-politics/afghanistans-neighbors-wary-as-us-seeks-nearby-staging-area/\" target=\"_blank\">new military facilities</a> in Central Asian countries to enable continued attacks on the Taliban and other militants in Afghanistan.</p><p>Military and other government leaders have long portrayed U.S. bases in the Middle East and other parts of the globe as defensive in nature, as deterrents, as forces of stability and peace. One need only look at the role U.S. bases abroad have played in <a href=\"https://www.ucpress.edu/book/9780520300873/the-united-states-of-war\" target=\"_blank\">launching</a> the last 20 years of offensive wars to question such claims. The history of U.S. wars since 1776 likewise reveals that when bases are occupying foreign lands, they are not likely to be defensive in nature. Bases abroad are generally offensive in nature. They are designed to threaten, to deploy military force, and to wage offensive wars. <a href=\"https://www.rand.org/pubs/research_reports/RR1906.html\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">Research</a> funded by the U.S. Army indicates that since the 1950s, a U.S. military presence abroad has been correlated with U.S. forces initiating military conflicts. In other words, there appears to be a relationship between establishing bases outside the United States and the incidence of wars.</p><h2>Military force: the main tool of U.S. foreign policy</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"4f17e339f58b2054a66261e7a10eeaad\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"c4e87\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODM0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM4NDU5N30.gg7A5J2aonBcbla16SR_ol2qENUZtaK1Z_mVVx2dZWY/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n        \n        \n        <small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\"> <a href=\"https://unsplash.com/photos/sbhubyl7lWI\" target=\"_blank\">Credit</a>: DON JACKSON-WYATT via Unsplash</small></p><p>U.S. history shows that bases beyond U.S. borders have made war and the deployment of military force too easy and too tempting for politicians, government officials, and other elites with the power to shape government decisions. Bases abroad have provided what is by design an easily deployable form of offensive military power. With this offensive power readily available, elites often have been tempted to advocate for its use to advance their own economic and political interests and the interests of fellow business leaders, politicians, and entrepreneurs.<br/></p><p>As anthropologist <a href=\"https://www.imdb.com/title/tt1543883/\" target=\"_blank\">Catherine Lutz</a> and others have said, bases and troops abroad long have been the main tool in the U.S. foreign policy toolbox. They have been the hammers that have left little room for diplomacy and other foreign policy tools. And when hammers dominate one's toolbox, Lutz says, everything starts looking like a nail. The hammers become all too tempting, especially when mostly male policymakers perceive them, consciously or unconsciously, as visible demonstrations of their masculinity and strength.</p><p>Notably, the historical record shows that U.S. wars often have led U.S. leaders to establish new bases abroad. The establishment of more bases abroad, in turn, has often led to more wars, which has often led to more bases, in a repeating pattern over time. Put another way, bases frequently beget wars, which can beget more bases, which can beget more wars, and so on.</p><p>By this, I do not just mean that the construction of bases abroad has enabled more war. I mean that the construction of bases abroad actually has made aggressive, offensive war <em>more likely.</em> Since the revolution that won independence from Britain, the construction and maintenance of extraterritorial bases has increased the likelihood that these bases would be used to wage wars of aggression. Riffing off the famous line from the baseball movie <em>Field of Dreams,</em> one might say, \"If we build them, wars will come.\" Except the reference is to bases rather than baseball: if we build bases abroad, wars likely will come. To be clear, bases abroad are not the sole cause of U.S. wars. Bases beyond U.S. borders are, however, a particularly<em> </em>important cause, along with forces related to imperialism, capitalism, racism, patriarchy, nationalism, and religious chauvinism.</p><p>Still, the \"if\" in \"If we build them, wars will come\" is an important reminder of the choices involved in both the history and the future of the United States' relationship to war. There was nothing inevitable about past U.S. wars. At times, U.S. leaders have avoided wars, often because of pressure from social movements and other protest. Today, there are choices to be made about the country's future.</p><h2>Does war even achieve our geopolitical objectives?</h2><p>The past 20 years of war have resulted in the deaths of <a href=\"https://watson.brown.edu/costsofwar/figures/2019/direct-war-death-toll-2001-801000\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">15,000</a> U.S. troops and hundreds of thousands wounded. Two decades of fighting have meant <a href=\"https://thehill.com/opinion/national-security/470128-reckoning-with-the-costs-of-war-its-time-to-take-responsibility\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">3 to 4 million or more</a> dead, on all sides, in Afghanistan, Iraq, Pakistan, Yemen, and Syria, alone. Tens of millions have been injured. At least <a href=\"http://www.costsofwar.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">38 million</a> have been displaced. For U.S. taxpayers, the costs of the war on terror will exceed <a href=\"http://www.costsofwar.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">$7 trillion</a>. Imagine what these trillions could have done to protect against pandemics, to provide universal healthcare, to end homelessness and hunger, to rebuild public schools, and to erase college debt. Meanwhile, the war on terror has been a failure on its own terms: responding to al Qaeda's attacks with war has created more organizations, such as the Islamic State, willing to use terror as a political tool.</p><p>Despite the catastrophe of the last 20 years of war, many government officials and other elites appear content to perpetuate the status quo. Many are undeterred by the pattern of moving from one catastrophic war to the next: from Vietnam to Afghanistan to Iraq to Libya to Syria to Yemen to…</p><p>With U.S. troops currently or recently engaged in combat in <a href=\"https://watson.brown.edu/costsofwar/papers/2021/USCounterterrorismOperations\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\">13 or more</a> countries, interventionist military and civilian officials are already planning for the next major war. A growing number of politicians, pundits, and think-tank analysts are talking openly about the inevitability of a future war with China. Such talk runs the risk of becoming a self-fulfilling prophecy and should terrify us all: a war between two nuclear armed powers and the two wealthiest nations on earth could dwarf the horrific death and destruction of the last two decades.</p><h2>Why is the U.S. military everywhere?</h2><p>While a new $1 billion military hospital rises in Germany, we should ask where else we could have built that hospital and how else we could have spent $1 billion. We should ask why there are any U.S. bases in Germany today, 30 years after the end of the Cold War and more than 75 years after the end of World War II. We should ask why U.S. troops are not withdrawing from the entire Middle East rather than Afghanistan alone.</p><p>Beyond asking questions, U.S. citizens and leaders can demand the country give up on offensive foreign bases and our long pattern of war. It may sound radical — though it should not — but we can demand a foreign policy built not around fighting but around diplomacy, international cooperation, violence reduction, and principles at the heart of the best traditions in U.S. history: democracy, equity, and justice.</p><p><em>David Vine is Professor of Anthropology at American University in Washington, DC. David is the author of a trilogy of books about war and peace including, </em><a href=\"https://www.ucpress.edu/book/9780520385689/the-united-states-of-war\" target=\"_blank\">The United States of War: A Global History of America's Endless Conflicts, from Columbus to the Islamic State</a><em>, which is out in paperback September 7. David is also the author of </em>Base Nation: How U.S. Military Bases Abroad Harm America and the World <em>and</em> Island of Shame: The Secret History of the U.S. Military on Diego Garcia.</p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/y7Qt5sBcrmk\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Thu, 12 Aug 2021 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":32,"bgimg":"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODM0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NDUxMTg1MH0.U0oUArcjhN2ttwJVrcecvjgwc8sXhvYdHh6H2zb-4TY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C18%2C0%2C184&height=700","linkMd5":"e269ba922a82ec7d4514097dade8d377","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn7@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-24-967_cad1f0a932a56b5e.webp","destWidth":1245,"destHeight":701,"sourceBytes":26808,"destBytes":26808,"author":"David Vine","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODM0MS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2NDUxMTg1MH0.U0oUArcjhN2ttwJVrcecvjgwc8sXhvYdHh6H2zb-4TY/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C18%2C0%2C184&height=700":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn7@2020_3/2021/08/15/03-53-24-967_cad1f0a932a56b5e.webp","https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE4ODM0NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY1MTM4NDU5N30.gg7A5J2aonBcbla16SR_ol2qENUZtaK1Z_mVVx2dZWY/img.jpg?width=980":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn23@2020_5/2021/08/15/03-53-28-111_c4e874252f75605a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/bigthink/main/~4/y7Qt5sBcrmk":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn32@2020_1/2021/08/15/03-53-27-992_8519dce25153353f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1628999588787},{"createdTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","updatedTime":"2021-08-15 11:53:08","title":"The strange case of the dead-but-not-dead Tibetan monks","link":"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/thukdam-study","description":"<img src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0MDU3NS9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2ODcxNzYwMn0.WU9_xn8ap53urg53vAJawsi4G3MD1pw2-uiZeWEhYak/img.jpg?width=1245&coordinates=0%2C0%2C0%2C0&height=700\"/><br/><br/><ul><li>The bodies of some Tibetan monks remain \"fresh\" after what appears to be their death.</li><li><span></span>Their fellow monks say they're not dead yet but in a deep, final meditative state called \"thukdam.\"</li><li>Science has not found any evidence of lingering EEG activity after death in thukdam monks.</li></ul><hr/><p>\n\tIt's definitely happening, and it's definitely weird. After the apparent death of some monks, their bodies remain in a meditating position without decaying for an extraordinary length of time, often as long as two or three weeks. A fascinating account of the phenomenon was written by Daniel Burke for the publication <a href=\"https://tricycle.org/trikedaily/thukdam-project/\" target=\"_blank\">Tricycle</a>.</p><p>\n\tTibetan Buddhists, who view death as a process rather than an event, might assert that the spirit has not yet finished with the physical body. For them, <em>thukdam</em> begins with a \"clear light\" meditation that allows the mind to gradually unspool, eventually dissipating into a state of universal consciousness no longer attached to the body. Only at that time is the body free to die.\n</p><p>\n\tWhether you believe this or not, it is a fascinating phenomenon: the fact remains that their bodies don't decompose like other bodies. (There have been a handful of <a href=\"https://www.mentalfloss.com/article/58170/5-bodies-refused-rot\" target=\"_blank\">other unexplained instances</a> of delayed decomposition elsewhere in the world.)\n</p><p>\n\tThe scientific inquiry into just what is going on with thukdam has attracted the attention and support of the Dalai Lama, the highest monk in Tibetan Buddhism. He has reportedly been looking for scientists to solve the riddle for about 20 years. He is a supporter of science,<a href=\"https://amzn.to/3icfHUg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> writing</a>, \"Buddhism and science are not conflicting perspectives on the world, but rather differing approaches to the same end: seeking the truth.\"\n</p><p>\n\tThe most serious study of the phenomenon so far is being undertaken by The Thukdam Project of the<a href=\"https://centerhealthyminds.org/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> University of Wisconsin-Madison's Center for Healthy Minds</a>. Neuroscientist Richard Davidson is one of the founders of the center and has published hundreds of articles about<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/videos/jon-kabat-zinn-on-mindfulness\" target=\"_self\"> mindfulness</a>.\n</p><p>\n\tDavidson first encountered thukdam after his Tibetan monk friend Geshe Lhundub Sopa died, officially on August 28, 2014. Davidson last saw him five days later: \"There was absolutely no change. It was really quite remarkable.\"\n</p><h2>The science so far</h2><p class=\"shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image\">\n        <img class=\"rm-shortcode\" data-rm-shortcode-id=\"7f87b952ddbaf72a65ecf7fd38c6bbec\" data-rm-shortcode-name=\"rebelmouse-image\" id=\"5b930\" loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://assets.rebelmouse.io/eyJhbGciOiJIUzI1NiIsInR5cCI6IkpXVCJ9.eyJpbWFnZSI6Imh0dHBzOi8vYXNzZXRzLnJibC5tcy8yNzE0MDc4NC9vcmlnaW4uanBnIiwiZXhwaXJlc19hdCI6MTY2OTU4MTUyNX0.BkN3apnldHCTqVdHnbXqUkYJ9C-4QdLE5awrN2sVHfU/img.jpg?width=980\"/>\n\t<small class=\"image-media media-photo-credit\" placeholder=\"Add Photo Credit...\">Credit:<a href=\"https://stock.adobe.com/contributor/207149505/michel?load_type=author&prev_url=detail\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> GrafiStart</a> / Adobe Stock</small>\n</p><p>The Thukdam Project published its first annual report this winter. It discussed a<a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7876463/\" target=\"_blank\"> recent study</a> in which electroencephalograms failed to detect any brain activity in 13 monks who had practiced thukdam and had been dead for at least 26 hours. Davidson was senior author of the study.<br/></p><p>\n\tWhile some might be inclined to say, well, that's that, Davidson sees the research as just a first step on a longer road. Philosopher<a href=\"https://tricycle.org/podcast/why-im-not-a-buddhist/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Evan Thompson</a>, who is not involved in The Thukdam Project, tells<a href=\"https://tricycle.org/trikedaily/thukdam-project/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> Tricycle</a>, \"If the thinking was that thukdam is something we can measure in the brain, this study suggests that's not the right place to look.\"\n</p><p>\n\tIn any event, the question remains: why are these apparently deceased monks so slow to begin decomposition? While environmental factors can slow or speed up the process a bit, usually decomposition begins about<a href=\"https://www.aftermath.com/content/human-decomposition/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\" target=\"_blank\"> four minutes</a> after death and becomes quite obvious over the course of the next day or so.\n</p><p>\n\tAs the Dalai Lama said:\n</p><p style=\"margin-left: 20px;\">\n\t\"What science finds to be nonexistent we should all accept as nonexistent, but what science merely does not find is a completely different matter. An example is consciousness itself. Although sentient beings, including humans, have experienced consciousness for centuries, we still do not know what consciousness actually is: its complete nature and how it functions.\"\n</p><h2>Consciousness</h2><p>\n\tAs thukdam researchers continue to seek a signal of post-mortem consciousness of some sort, it's fair to ask what — and where — consciousness is in the first place. It is a question with which<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/mind-brain/consciousness\" target=\"_self\"> Big Think</a> readers are familiar. We write about new theories all the time: consciousness happens on a<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/surprising-science/quantum-consciousness\" target=\"_self\"> quantum level</a>; consciousness<a href=\"https://bigthink.com/philip-perry/the-universe-may-be-conscious-prominent-scientists-state\" target=\"_self\"> is everywhere</a>.\n</p><p>\n\tSo far, though, says Tibetan medical doctor Tawni Tidwell, also a Thukdam Project member, searches beyond the brain for signs of consciousness have gone nowhere.
